The Maxim 'Al-Ma'ruf Ka Al-Mashrut': Custom as Stipulation
Introduces the maxim with the dyer case and analogous applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' to identify when custom substitutes for an explicit condition in Hanafi mu'amalat.

Core Idea
Custom fills the silence in contracts — what is customary is legally equivalent to what is stipulated.

Imagine giving cloth to a dyer without discussing payment. Does custom step in? The Hanafi maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' (custom is like stipulation) answers yes, but only when the custom is firmly established. In this lesson, you will explore the dyer case, the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and how the maxim generalizes to craftsmen, inns, and brokers.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its plain meaning
Begin by reading the maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' and understand its implication: a well-known customary practice in a transaction has the same legal effect as if it were explicitly agreed upon.
This maxim is a furu' (branch) of the larger qa'ida 'al-'adah muhakkamah'.
2
Analyze the dyer case and the three Hanafi positions
Identify the scenario: a man gives cloth to a dyer without specifying a fee. Then examine the positions of Abu Hanifa (no fee), Abu Yusuf (fee if regular customer), and Muhammad (fee if dyer is publicly known for the trade).
Each imam weighs the evidentiary value of custom differently.
3
Identify the mufta bihi and the tarjih
Al-Zayla'i states that the fatwa is on Muhammad's view. Understand why: Muhammad's condition of public reputation strikes a balance between strictness and practicality.
The mufta bihi relies on the most widely accepted customary practice.
4
Generalize the principle to other craftsmen and services
The text explicitly extends the ruling to any craftsman who sets himself up for work (e.g., inns, bathhouses, brokers). Recognize that silence in such contexts is treated as consent to the customary fee.
The key condition is that the service is 'prepared for exploitation' (ma'add li al-istighlal).
5
Apply the maxim to analogous cases from the furuat
Work through the examples: staying at a caravanserai, entering a hammam, hiring a broker. For each, confirm that the mufta bihi ruling aligns with Muhammad's standard: the worker must be known for charging a fee.
These cases are taken from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Multaqat, confirming the maxim's broad application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى صباغ ليصبغه له ولم يعين له أجرة، ثم اختلفا في الأجر وعدمه وقد جرت العادة بالعمل بالأجرة؛ فهل ينزل منزلة شرط الأجرة؟ فيه اختلاف قال الإمام الأعظم: لا أجرة له، وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله: إن كان الصابغ حريفًا له أي معاملاً له فله الأجر وإلا لا. وقال محمد رحمه الله: إن كان الصابغ معروفًا بهذه الصنعة بالأجر وقيام حاله بها كان القول قوله وإلا فلا اعتبارًا للظاهر المعتاد. وقال الزيلعي: والفتوى على قول محمد رحمه الله. (انتهى). ولا خصوصية لصابغ بل كل صانع نصب نفسه للعمل بأجرة فإن السكوت كالاشتراط. ومن هذا القبيل نزول الخان ودخول الحمام والدلال كما في البزازية. هذا القبيل المعدّ للاستغلال كما في الملتقط. ولذا قالوا المعروف كالمشروط، فعلى المفتى به صارت عادته كالمشروط صريحًا.
To a dyer to dye for him, and he did not specify a fee, then they differed on the fee and its absence, and custom had been established to work for a fee; so does it take the place of stipulating the fee? There is disagreement: Imam al-A'zam said: no fee for him. Abu Yusuf said: if the dyer is his regular customer, i.e., one who deals with him, then he has the fee, otherwise not. Muhammad said: if the dyer is known for this trade for a fee and his condition is such, then his word is accepted; otherwise not, considering the apparent custom. Al-Zayla'i said: the fatwa is on Muhammad's statement. (end). The ruling is not specific to a dyer, but every craftsman who sets himself up to work for a fee — silence is like stipulation. Among this category is staying at a caravanserai, entering a bathhouse, and the broker, as in al-Bazzaziyya. This category is prepared for exploitation, as in al-Multaqat. Hence they said: what is customary is like what is stipulated; according to the mufta bih, his custom becomes like an explicit stipulation.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces the foundational maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' (the customary is like the stipulated). It begins with a concrete case: a man gives cloth to a dyer without specifying a fee. When they later dispute whether a fee is owed, the scholars differ. Imam Abu Hanifa holds no fee, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani give conditions based on the dyer's regular dealings or public reputation. Al-Zayla'i affirms that the fatwa is on Muhammad's view, adopting the customary practice as decisive. The text then generalizes: any craftsman who openly works for a fee is presumed to have a claim by silence, just as if he had stipulated. Analogous cases include staying at an inn, using a bathhouse, and hiring a broker—all are understood to be for compensation by custom. The maxim is stated explicitly: 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut,' and the relied-upon fatwa makes custom equivalent to an explicit condition.

Key Terms
المعروف (al-ma'ruf): Customary practice that is well-known and established in a particular context
المشروط (al-mashrut): An explicit stipulation or condition that parties agree upon

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa, when does a dyer get a fee if no fee was mentioned?
A) Always, because any worker deserves pay.
B) Only if the dyer is a regular customer of the owner.
C) If the dyer is publicly known to work for a fee and his condition shows that.
D) Never, because without an agreement there is no obligation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a well-established custom in trade effectively operates as a contractual stipulation, but only when the worker is publicly known for the trade.

Two Unresolved Hypotheticals on Custom as Stipulation
Two hypothetical derivations from the custom-as-stipulation maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mashrut 'urfan ka al-mashrut shar'an' to derive rulings in novel cases, and identify when a custom becomes equivalent to a stipulation.

Core Idea
Custom can turn an implied practice into a binding stipulation, affecting rulings like riba and amnesty.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents two hypothetical cases that challenge the mufti: when does a custom become so strong it is like an explicit condition? The first involves a borrower who habitually gives extra, the second a battlefield custom of amnesty. These are not given answers, but the method of derivation is clear. The maxim 'al-mashrut 'urfan ka al-mashrut shar'an' from al-Bazzaziyya provides the framework.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Recite the maxim 'al-mashrut 'urfan ka al-mashrut shar'an' and its English meaning: 'What is stipulated by custom is like what is stipulated by law.' Understand that it treats a pervasive custom as an implied condition.
This is a minor (sughra) legal maxim in the Hanafi school, governing mu'amalat.
2
Identify the custom in each case
For the first case: the borrower customarily repays more than the principal. For the second: it is customary to grant amnesty to a kafir warrior who steps forward to face a Muslim. Note that these are established practices, not one-off occurrences.
The custom must be general and consistent to be considered like a stipulation.
3
Perform the equivalence analysis
Ask: Is the custom so strong and unambiguous that it effectively functions as an explicit stipulation? If the borrower always gives extra, the lender's expectation becomes a de facto condition. Similarly, if amnesty is always given, the kafir relies on that custom as a promise of safety.
The key is whether the custom creates a legal expectation equivalent to a contractual term.
4
Derive the ruling
If the custom is equivalent to a stipulation, then: 1) Lending becomes prohibited because the extra payment becomes riba due to the implied condition. 2) Helping the Muslim against the kafir becomes prohibited because the amnesty custom creates a binding covenant.
Note that these are derivations, not settled fatwas—the author says 'I have not seen them now.'
5
Note the uncertainty
Recognize that these are hypothetical extensions. The mufti must evaluate the strength of the custom. Not every customary practice rises to the level of a stipulation; it must be equivalent in certainty and effect.
This is a key point for iftaa: custom is not automatically binding; it must be analyzed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا مسألتان لم أرهما الآن يمكن تخريجهما على أن المعروف كالمشروط، وفي البزازية المشروط عُرْفًا كالمشروط شرعًا منها لو جرت عادة المقترض بردّ أزيد مما اقترض هل يحرم إقراضه تنزيلاً لعادته بمنزلة الشرط؟ ومنها لو بارز كافر مسلما واطردت العادة بالأمان للكافر، هل يكون بمنزلة اشتراط الأمان له فيحرم على المسلمين إعانة المسلم عليه؟
And here are two issues I have not seen now, but they can be derived on the basis that custom is like a stipulation. In al-Bazzaziyya: what is stipulated by custom is like what is stipulated by law. Among them: if a borrower customarily repays more than he borrowed, does it become prohibited to lend to him, treating his custom as equivalent to a stipulation? And among them: if a disbeliever comes out to face a Muslim in battle, and the custom is established that the disbeliever is given amnesty, does that become like stipulating amnesty for him, thus prohibiting Muslims from helping the Muslim against him?
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents two unresolved hypotheticals that can be derived from the maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut'. The first concerns a borrower who customarily returns more than he borrowed. If the custom of giving extra is so strong that it is effectively stipulated, then lending would be prohibited because it resembles riba with an implied condition. The second concerns a battlefield scenario: if a kafir warrior steps forward and it is customary to grant him amnesty, does this custom become like an explicit promise of safety? If so, it would be haram for other Muslims to help the opposing Muslim against him. The text notes these as derivations from al-Bazzaziyya's statement that what is stipulated by custom is like a legal stipulation. No definitive answer is given here, showing that the jurists considered these as open questions.

Key Terms
المشروط عُرْفًا (al-mashrut 'urfan): A condition that is established by custom, even if not explicitly stated
المعروف كالمشروط (al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut): What is customary is like what is stipulated

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why might lending be prohibited if a borrower customarily returns extra?
A) Because any extra payment is riba.
B) Because the custom becomes like a stipulation, making the loan identical to a riba contract.
C) Because the lender intends the extra.
D) Because the borrower has a habit.

Key Takeaway
Custom can create an implied stipulation, but only when it is consistent and strong enough to be equivalent to an explicit condition.

Custom as Stipulation: Rental and Loan Guarantee Applications
Resolves a mill rental case and discusses loan guarantee by custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' to a rental custom case and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on stipulating guarantee in a loan ('ariyah).

Core Idea
What is customary is like a stipulation — custom binds where no explicit condition exists.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents two distinct applications of a core Hanafi legal maxim. First, a real-world query about liability for clay pots in a rented sugar mill is resolved by custom. Second, the theoretical question of whether stipulating guarantee in a loan ('ariyah) makes the borrower liable is examined through two conflicting narrations. Together, these cases illustrate both the power and limits of custom in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim at work
What is the key qawida in this passage? How is it phrased? Locate 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' and understand its meaning: customary practice is legally equivalent to an explicit stipulation.
Maxim is stated in the response to the query.
2
Analyze the rental case
Describe the scenario: a mill rented for sugar cooking includes clay pots; custom in mills holds the tenant liable for such pots. Apply the maxim to derive the ruling.
Custom here fills a contractual gap.
3
Examine the loan dispute
Identify the two narrations: one from al-Zayla'i/al-Jawhara that stipulating guarantee in 'ariyah makes it guaranteed, and one from al-Bazzaziyya that it does not. Note the basis of each.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; no tarjih is given.
4
Apply the maxim to the loan
Consider whether 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' applies here. In the loan case, there is an explicit condition, not custom. The ikhtilaf is about the effect of the condition itself, not custom.
The maxim is not directly used in the loan dispute; it serves as a contrast.
5
Observe the limits of custom
Note the explicit exceptions: wadi'ah (deposit) and leased property (al-'ayn al-mu'jara) are never guaranteed by any condition. This shows that custom cannot override the inherent nature of certain contracts.
These are istithna'at to the general domain of the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحين تأليف هذا المحل ورد علي سؤال فيمن أجر مطبحًا لطبخ السكر وفيه فخار، أذِنَ للمستأجر في استعمالها فتلف ذلك وقد جرى العُرف في المطابخ بضمانها على المستأجر، فأجبت بأن المعروف كالمشروط فصار كأنه صرح بضمانها عليه. والعارية إذا اشترط فيها الضمان على المستعير تصير مضمونة عندنا في رواية، ذكره الزيلعي في العارية وجزم به في الجوهرة ولم يقل في رواية لكن نقل بعده فرع البزازية عن الينابيع ثم قال: وأما الوديعة والعين المؤجرة فلا يضمنان بحال. (اهـ). ولكن في البزازية قال: أعرني هذا على أنه إن ضاع فأنا ضامن له فأعاره فضاع لم يضمن. (اهـ).
And at the time of compiling this work, a question came to me regarding someone who rented a mill for cooking sugar, and in it were clay pots. He permitted the tenant to use them, and they were destroyed. Custom in mills holds the tenant liable for them. So I answered: what is customary is like what is stipulated, so it is as if he explicitly made him liable. And regarding 'ariyah (loan): if guarantee is stipulated on the borrower, it becomes guaranteed according to us in one narration. Al-Zayla'i mentioned it in al-'Ariyah and affirmed it in al-Jawhara without saying 'in a narration', but he afterwards transmitted a branch from al-Bazzaziyya from al-Yanabi' and then said: as for wadi'ah (deposit) and the leased object (al-'ayn al-mu'jara), they are never guaranteed. (end). But in al-Bazzaziyya he said: lend me this on condition that if it is lost I am liable, so he lent him and it was lost: he is not liable. (end).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage gives two distinct applications of the maxim. First, a real query: a tenant rents a sugar mill that includes clay pots; custom holds the tenant liable for damage to such pots. The author answers by applying 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut', making the tenant liable by custom as if he had stipulated it. Second, a discussion on 'ariyah (loan for use): if the borrower agrees to guarantee the item, does he become liable? There are two narrations: one says yes, the other (from al-Bazzaziyya) says no, even if he promised to guarantee. The author notes that wadi'ah (deposit) and leased property are never guaranteed regardless. This shows the limit of the maxim: not all arrangements can be transformed by a condition.

Key Terms
العارية (al-'ariyah): A loan of an object for use without compensation; the borrower is normally not liable for accidental loss

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the exception mentioned in the passage regarding contracts that are never guaranteed by condition?
A) Rental and loan.
B) Wadiah (deposit) and lease (al-'ayn al-mu'jara).
C) Mill and sugar.
D) All contracts.

Key Takeaway
Custom operates like a stipulation, but its effect is limited by the inherent nature of certain contracts (like trust contracts) where explicit conditions also face ikhtilaf.

Father's Dowry and the Role of Custom
Applies custom maxim to father's dowry and other transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' to resolve disputes over the nature of a father's dowry gift, and identify additional furuat where custom determines the ruling.

Core Idea
When a father gives dowry to his daughter, custom decides whether it is a gift or a loan—the consistent 'urf governs.

This lesson examines a key legal maxim in Hanafi fiqh: 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut'—custom is like a stipulation. Through the case of a father's dowry (jihaz), you will see how custom resolves disputes when evidence is lacking. The passage presents multiple scholarly opinions and then extends the principle to sales, hiring, and other transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
State the maxim: al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut. 'Ma'ruf' refers to established custom. This is a wusta (intermediate) principle governing many fiqh chapters where text or explicit agreement is absent.
The maxim is derived from the Quranic principle of 'urf and from the practice of the Companions.
2
Analyze the primary fur': father's dowry
The passage presents three opinions: (a) consistent custom determines ownership; (b) Qadi Khan looks to the father's social status; (c) al-Kubra places burden on the father after wife's death. Identify which is mufta bih.
Mufta bih is the first: custom of the town.
3
Apply the maxim to the primary case
Using the mufta bih, determine the ruling: if custom consistently shows fathers give ownership, the father's claim of loan is rejected; if custom is mixed, his word is accepted. No evidence is needed; custom fills the gap.
This is the direct application of 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut'.
4
Explore additional furuat
The passage applies custom to: market goods (presumption of halal), animal sale accessories (pack-saddle/halter included by custom), hire of a child to learn trade (wages determined by custom), guards' wages divided among all beneficiaries, and yarn for weaving for a share of output. For each, confirm how custom determines the ruling.
These examples show the breadth of the maxim across different fiqh chapters.
5
Synthesize the principle
Understand that custom operates as a contextual default when text or explicit agreement is absent. The mufti must ascertain the 'urf of the specific time and place. The maxim provides a framework for ijtihad.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما تفرع على أن المعروف كالمشروط لو جهز الأب بنته جهازا ودفعه لها ثم ادعى أنه عارية ولا بيّنة. ففيه اختلاف؛ والفتوى أنه إن كان العرف مستمرًا أن الأب يدفع ذلك الجهاز ملكًا لا عارية لم يقبل قوله، وإن كان العرف مشتركا فالقول للأب؛ كذا في شرح منظومة ابن وهبان وقال قاضي خان وعندي أن الأب إن كان من كرام الناس وأشرافهم لم يقبل قوله، وإن كان من أوساط الناس كان القول قوله (اهـ). وفي الكبرى للخاصي أن القول للزوج بعد موتها وعلى الأب البينة لأن الظاهر شاهد للزوج كمَن دفع ثوبًا إلى قصار ليقصره ولم يذكر الأجر فإنه يحمل على الإجارة بشهادة الظاهر. (اهـ). (1) وعلى كل قول فالمنظور إليه العُرْف؛ فالقول المُفتی به نظر إلى عُرف بلدهما، وقاضيخان نظر إلى حال الأب في العُرْف، وما في الكبرى نظر إلى مطلق العُرْف من أن الأب إنما يجهز ملكا، من البيوع وعن أب القاسم الصفار: الأشياء الملتقط وفي على ظاهر ما جرت به العادة؛ فإن كان الغالب الحلال في الأسواق لا يجب السؤال، وإن كان الغالب الحرام في وقت أو كان الرجل يأخذ المال من حيث وجده ولا يتأمل في الحلال والحرام فالسؤال عنه حسن. اهـ). وفيه أيضًا أن دخول البرذعة الحمار مبني على العُرف، وفيه أيضًا أن حمل الأجير الأحمال إلى والإكاف في بيع داخل الباب مبني على التعارف ذكره في الإجارات. وفي إجارات منية المفتي: رجل دفع غلامه إلى حائك مدة معلومة ليتعلم النسج ولم يشترط الأجر على أحد فلما علم العمل طلب الأستاذ الأجر من المولى والمولى من الأستاذ ينظر إلى عُرف أهل تلك البلدة في ذلك العمل فإن كان العُرْف يشهد للأستاذ يحكم بأجر مثل تعليم ذلك العمل على المولى وإن كان يشهد للمولى فأجر مثل ذلك الغلام على الأستاذ وكذلك لو دفع ابنه. (اهـ). ومما بنوه على العُرْف أن أكثر أهل السوق إذا استأجروا حراسًا وكره الباقون فإن الأجرة تؤخذ من الكل، وكذا في منافع القرية وتمامه في منية المفتي، وفيها لو دفع غزلاً إلى حائك لينسجه بالنصف جوزه مشایخ بخارى وأبو الليث وغيره للعرف. (اهـ).
Among the branches derived from the principle that custom is like a stipulation: if a father equips his daughter with a dowry and gives it to her, then claims it was a loan and there is no evidence, there is disagreement. The fatwa is: if the consistent custom is that fathers give such dowry as ownership, not loan, his claim is not accepted; if custom is mixed, his word is accepted. This is in Sharh Manzumat Ibn Wahban. Qadi Khan said: in my view, if the father is among the noble and honorable, his claim is not accepted; if he is among the common people, his word is accepted. In al-Kubra by al-Khasi: the word is with the husband after her death, and the burden of proof is on the father, because the apparent circumstance witnesses for the husband, like one who gives cloth to a fuller to full it without mentioning the fee; it is held to be hire by the witness of the apparent. (end). (1) In any case, the consideration is custom; the mufta bih opinion looks to the custom of their town; Qadi Khan looks to the father's status in custom; and what is in al-Kubra looks to absolute custom: that fathers give dowry as ownership. From the sales chapter: Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar said: things found are to be treated according to what custom shows; if the majority in markets is halal, inquiry is not required; if majority is haram at a time, or if a person takes money from wherever he finds it without considering halal and haram, then inquiry is good. Also in it: the inclusion of the pack-saddle and the donkey's halter in the sale is based on custom. Also in it: the porter's carrying loads and the pack-saddle in sale inside the door is based on common practice, mentioned in Ijarah. In the Ijarah section of Munyat al-Mufti: a man gives his slave to a weaver for a known period to learn weaving, and does not stipulate a fee on anyone; when he learns, the teacher demands wages from the master, and the master from the teacher; one looks to the custom of the people of that town in that work; if custom testifies for the teacher, he is awarded the like wage for teaching from the master; if it testifies for the master, then the like wage of that slave is on the teacher; similarly if he gives his son. (end). Among what they built on custom: if most of the market people hire guards and the rest refuse, the wages are taken from all; similarly for the benefits of a village; its completion is in Munyat al-Mufti. Also in it: if one gives yarn to a weaver to weave it for half (the cloth), the scholars of Bukhara and Abu al-Layth and others permitted it due to custom. (end).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This long passage applies the maxim to a common family law case: a father gives his daughter a dowry (jihaz) at marriage. Later he claims it was a loan (`ariyah), not a gift. Since there is no evidence, the dispute is resolved by custom. Three approaches are presented: (1) The fatwa from Sharh Manzumat Ibn Wahban distinguishes between consistent custom (fathers always give as ownership) - then father's claim is rejected; if custom is mixed, his claim is accepted. (2) Qadi Khan looks to the father's social status: if he is from the nobility, his claim is not accepted (since nobles don't lend dowries); if from commoners, his word is accepted. (3) Al-Kubra favors the husband after the wife's death, putting the burden on the father, analogizing to the case of a fuller where the apparent circumstance implies hire. The passage then notes that the operative principle is custom - the mufta bih considers the custom of the town, Qadi Khan considers the father's status within custom, and al-Kubra considers absolute custom that fathers give as ownership. The passage continues with other examples of custom in sales and hiring: market goods presumed halal if mostly halal, the inclusion of accessories in sales of animals, and the division of wages for guards among all beneficiaries. It ends with permission given by Bukhara scholars to have cloth woven for a share of the output, based on custom.

Key Terms
الجهاز (al-jihaz): Dowry or trousseau given by the father to the daughter upon marriage
المعروف (al-ma'ruf): Established custom, common practice
المشروط (al-mashrut): Stipulated condition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bih position when a father claims his dowry gift was a loan and there is no evidence?
A) The father's word is always accepted.
B) The husband always wins because the property was in the wife's possession.
C) One looks to the custom of their town: if custom consistently shows fathers give ownership, the father's claim is rejected; if custom is mixed, his word is accepted.
D) The father's social status determines: if noble, his claim rejected; if commoner, accepted.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'custom is like a stipulation' governs disputes where evidence is absent; the mufti must ascertain the actual custom of the people in that time and place.

Custom as the Decisive Factor in Contracts
Discussing the role of custom in determining contractual obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'المنظور إليه العرف' to resolve ambiguous contractual obligations and identify which custom is relied upon for fatwa.

Core Idea
In the absence of explicit terms, custom governs contractual obligations.

Custom ('urf) is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school, especially when contracts lack explicit terms. This lesson explores the maxim 'المنظور إليه العرف' (consideration is given to custom) and its practical applications in hire, sale, and partnership. The passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair illustrates how custom determines obligations in cases like giving cloth to a washer without mentioning wages or sending a slave to learn weaving. We will also examine differing views among Hanafi jurists on which custom to consider.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual ambiguity
Determine the point of contention: missing terms regarding wages, ownership, or scope of work.
e.g., cloth given to washer with no wage agreed.
2
Check for explicit text (nass)
If there is an explicit textual ruling from Quran, sunnah, or ijma, it takes precedence.
In these examples, no explicit text exists.
3
Determine the relevant custom
Identify the custom that applies: local custom (mufta bihi), personal custom (Qadikhan), or absolute custom (al-Kubra). The mufta bihi view is the custom of the parties' locality.
4
Apply the custom to the case
Use the prevailing custom to infer the missing terms or obligations.
e.g., if custom assumes wages, treat as ijarah; if custom assumes halves, permit halves.
5
Derive the ruling and state the fatwa
Based on the custom, give the operative ruling (mufta bihi) with consideration of local practice.
Include any exceptions or cautionary notes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (اهـ). وفي الكبرى للخاصي أن القول للزوج بعد موتها وعلى الأب البينة لأن الظاهر شاهد للزوج كمَن دفع ثوبًا إلى قصار ليقصره ولم يذكر الأخر فإنه يحمل على الإجارة بشهادة الظاهر . (اهـ).
(1)
وعلى كل قول فالمنظور إليه العُرْف؛ فالقول المُفتی به نظر إلى عُرف بلدهما، وقاضيخان نظر إلى حال الأب في العُرْف، وما في الكبرى نظر إلى مطلق العُرْف من أن الأب إنما يجهز ملكا، من البيوع وعن أب القاسم الصفار : الأشياء الملتقط وفي على ظاهر ما جرت به العادة؛ فإن كان الغالب الحلال في الأسواق لا يجب السؤال، وإن كان الغالب الحرام في وقت أو كان الرجل يأخذ المال من حيث وجده ولا يتأمل في الحلال والحرام فالسؤال عنه حسن . اهـ). وفيه أيضًا أن دخول البرذعة الحمار مبني على العُزف، وفيه أيضًا أن حمل الأجير الأحمال إلى والإكاف في بيع داخل الباب مبني على التعارف ذكره في الإجارات. وفي إجارات منية المفتي: رجل دفع غلامه إلى حائك مدة معلومة ليتعلم النسج ولم يشترط الأخر على أحد فلما علم العمل طلب الأستاذ الأجر من المولى والمولى من الأستاذ ينظر إلى عُزف أهل تلك البلدة في ذلك العمل فإن كان العُرْف يشهد للأستاذ يحكم بأجر مثل تعليم ذلك العمل على المولى وإن كان يشهد للمولى فأجر مثل ذلك الغلام على الأستاذ وكذلك لو دفع ابنه . (اهـ).
ومما بنوه على العُرْف أن أكثر أهل السوق إذا استأجروا حراسًا وكره الباقون فإن الأجرة تؤخذ من الكل، وكذا في منافع القرية وتمامه في منية المفتي، وفيها لو دفع غزلاً إلى حائك لينسجه بالنصف جوزه مشایخ بخارى وأبو الليث وغيره لِلْعُرْفِ . (اهـ).
His statement (end). In al-Kubra by al-Khassafi: the saying is the husband's after her death, and the father has the burden of proof, because the apparent circumstance is a witness for the husband, like one who gave cloth to a washer to wash it and did not mention the wage, for it is construed as hire by the witness of the apparent. (End). (1) In every statement, the consideration is custom; the mufta bihi statement looks to the custom of their country, Qadikhan looks to the custom of the father's situation, and what is in al-Kubra looks to absolute custom that the father only equips as ownership. From sales and from Abul Qasim al-Saffar: found items are considered according to the apparent custom; if the majority in markets is permissible, questioning is not required, but if the majority is forbidden at a time, or a man takes money from wherever he finds it without reflecting on halal and haram, then questioning about it is good. (End). Also in it: the inclusion of the pack-saddle for a donkey is based on custom, and also in it: the hired worker carrying loads and the pack-saddle in a sale inside the door is based on mutual recognition mentioned in Ijarat. In Ijarat of Minyat al-Mufti: a man gave his slave to a weaver for a known period to learn weaving and did not specify the wage on anyone; when he learned the work, the teacher demanded wages from the master and the master from the teacher; one looks to the custom of the people of that town in that work. If custom testifies for the teacher, he is awarded the equivalent wage for teaching that work from the master; if it testifies for the master, then the equivalent wage of such a slave is on the teacher. Similarly if he gave his son. (End). Among what they built on custom: if most of the market people hire guards and the rest refuse, the wage is taken from all. Similarly for the benefits of the village, and its completion in Minyat al-Mufti. In it: if one gives yarn to a weaver to weave it by halves, the shaykhs of Bukhara, Abu al-Layth and others permitted it due to custom. (End).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of custom ('urf) in determining legal rulings in contracts and transactions, particularly when the parties' intentions are not explicit. The author opens by citing a previous statement from al-Kubra al-Khassafi regarding a dispute between a husband and father after the wife's death, where the apparent circumstance (zahir) is a witness for the husband, analogous to giving cloth to a washer without mentioning wages—the default is hire. The author then emphasizes that the governing principle is custom ('urf). Three views are presented: (1) the mufta bihi view considers the custom of the parties' locality, (2) Qadikhan considers the custom of the father's situation, and (3) the view in al-Kubra considers absolute custom that fathers only provide equipment as ownership (i.e., gifts of property, not loan). Examples follow: in buying and selling, evaluating found items, and when to ask about halal/haram in markets—if the majority is halal, no need to ask; if haram dominates, questioning is recommended. The passage then gives specific rulings: the inclusion of a pack-saddle (bardha'a) for a donkey in a sale is based on custom, as is the hired worker's carrying of loads. The key example from Minyat al-Mufti involves a slave given to a weaver to learn weaving without mentioning wages. After the slave learns, the teacher demands wages from the master, and the master demands wages from the teacher. The ruling is to look at the custom of the town: if custom favors the teacher, he gets the equivalent teaching wage from the master; if custom favors the master, the equivalent slave wage is on the teacher. Similarly if a son is given. Another example: if most market people hire guards and some refuse, the custom is that the wage is shared by all. Similarly for village benefits. Finally, giving yarn to a weaver to weave on halves was permitted by the shaykhs of Bukhara, Abu al-Layth, and others due to custom. All these cases show that custom is a determinative factor in contractual obligations and their interpretation.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-zahir): The apparent or external circumstance that serves as evidence for a legal presumption
العرف (al-'urf): The prevailing custom or practice of a particular group or locality that influences legal rulings when there is no explicit text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Consider the custom of the father's situation
B) Consider absolute custom that the father only equips as ownership
C) Consider the custom of the parties' locality
D) Consider the custom of the market majority

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) is a determinative factor in contracts when explicit terms are missing, and the operative Hanafi position is to rely on the local custom of the parties.

Custom in Acknowledgment, Lawsuits, and Endowments
Distinguishing how custom applies to acknowledgment and lawsuits versus contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the governing qawida for custom in acknowledgment and lawsuits, apply it to distinguish between reports and contracts, and state the mufta bihi in cases of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Lawsuit and acknowledgment report the past; later custom does not interpret them. Contracts are initiated now, so custom applies.

This lesson unpacks a key qawida from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir that delineates the boundary of custom in legal statements. While custom ('urf) is generally authoritative in Hanafi fiqh, it does not override the nature of a statement. Acknowledgment (iqrar) and lawsuit (da'wa) are reports of past events; therefore, later custom cannot reinterpret them. Contracts, being immediate, are subject to custom. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti when dealing with ambiguous specifications in debts or currencies.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Reproduce the qawida in Arabic and English. Note that it divides legal statements into two categories: reports (iqrar/da'wa) and contracts ('aqd).
The key phrase is 'إخبار بما تقدم' (reports of what preceded).
2
Identify the scope and the dabt
The maxim governs interpretation in acknowledgment and lawsuits, particularly financial matters. The sub-maxim (dabt) is: 'Later custom is irrelevant in iqrar and da'wa.'
Contracts are excluded from this restriction.
3
Work through the furuat
Apply the qawida to the three cases: (1) acknowledging dirhams then saying counterfeit; (2) acknowledging usurpation/deposit then saying counterfeit; (3) lawsuit for unspecified currency when multiple types exist. Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf in the first furu'.
The istithna' (red dinars) also illustrates a limitation when a specification is already present.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf
Compare Imam Abu Hanifa's position with that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Identify the basis and determine the mufta bihi.
Mufta bihi is the view of the two for connected statements.
5
Extend to modern fatwa scenarios
Use the qawida to derive rulings on waqf stipends (batata) and qadi supervision. These show how the timing of custom matters.
Endowments made before a custom are not subject to it; only those after.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا وأما الإقرار فهو إخبار عن وجوب سابق وربما يقدّم الوجوب على العُرْف الغالب وكذا لو أقرَّ بدراهم ثم فسَّرها أنها زيوف أو تبهرجة يصدق إن وصل. وإن أقر بألف من ثمن متاع أو قرض لم يصدق عند الإمام إذا قال هي زيوف وصل أو فصل وصدقاه إن وصل وإن أقرَّ بألف غصبًا أو وديعة ثم قال زيوف صدق هي مطلقا. وكذا الدعوى لا تنزل على العادة لأن الدعوى والإقرار إخبار بما تقدم يقيده العُزف المتأخر بخلاف العقد فإنه باشره للحال فقيَّده العرف. قال في البزازية من الدعوى معزيا إلى اللامشي إذا كانت النقود في البلد مختلفة أحدها أروج لا تصح الدعوى ما لم يبين وكذا لو أقرَّ بعشرة دنانير حمر وفي البلد نقود مختلفة حمر تصح بلا بیان، بخلاف البيع فإنه ينصرف إلى الأروج . (انتهى) .
وقد أوسعنا الكلام على ذلك في شرح الكنز من أول البيع. ويمكن أن تخرج عليها مسألتان :إحداهما مسألة البطالة في المدارس فإذا استمر عرف بها في أشهر مخصوصة حمل عليها ما وقف بعدها لا ما وقف قبلها الثانية : إذا شرط الواقف النظر للحاكم وكان الحاكم إذ ذاك شافعيًا ثم صار الآن حنفيا لا قاضي غيره إلا نيابة . هل يكون النظر له لأنه الحاكم أولاً لأنه متأخر فلا يحمل المتقدم عليه؟ فمقتضى القاعدة الثاني. ولكن قالوا في الأيمان: لو حلفه والي بلدة ليعلمه بكل داعر دخل البلدة بطلت اليمين بعزل الوالي فلا يحنث إذا لم يعلم الوالي الثاني.
ولم أرَ الآن حكم ما إذا حلف متى رأى منكرًا رفعه إلى القاضي؛ هل تعين القاضي حالة اليمين؟ ومن هذا النوع لو وقف بلدا على الحرم الشريف وشرط النظر للقاضي هل ينصرف إلى قاضي الحرم أو قاضي البلدة الموقوفة أو قاضي بلد الواقف؟ ينبغي أن يستخرج من مسألة ما لو كان اليتيم في بلد وماله في بلد آخر فهل النظر عليه لقاضي بلد اليتيم أو لقاضي بلد ماله؟ صرَّحوا بالأول فينبغي أن يكون النظر لقاضي
But as for acknowledgment (iqrar), it is an informing of a prior obligation, and perhaps the obligation precedes the dominant custom. Similarly, if one acknowledges dirhams and then interprets them as counterfeit (zuyuf) or bad (tabharuja), he is truthful if he connects (wasala). If he acknowledges a thousand from the price of goods or a loan, he is not believed according to Imam Abu Hanifa if he says they are counterfeit, whether connected or disconnected; but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) believe him if connected. If he acknowledges a thousand as usurpation or deposit then says they are counterfeit, he is believed absolutely. Similarly, a lawsuit (da'wa) is not interpreted according to custom, because lawsuit and acknowledgment inform about something past, which is restricted by later custom, unlike a contract which is initiated immediately, so it is restricted by custom. He said in al-Bazzaziyya, citing al-Lamishi: If currencies in the country are different and one is more current, a lawsuit is not valid unless specified. Similarly, if one acknowledges ten red dinars (dhananir humr) and in the country there are different red currencies, it is valid without specification, unlike a sale, which refers to the most current. (End). We have elaborated on this in Sharh al-Kanz from the beginning of Buyu'. Two issues can be derived: First, the issue of stipends (batata) in schools: if a custom has been established in specific months, it applies to endowments made after that custom, not to endowments made before. Second: If the founder stipulated supervision for the qadi, and at that time the qadi was Shafi'i, then now he is Hanafi, and there is no other qadi except a deputy. Does the supervision go to him because he is the qadi, or since he is later (different school) so the earlier is not applied to him? The principle requires the second. But they said in oaths: If the ruler of a town made him swear to inform him of every troublemaker entering the town, the oath becomes void by the ruler's dismissal, so he does not break the oath if he does not inform the second ruler. I have not seen the ruling of one who swore that whenever he sees an evil he will raise it to the qadi: does the qadi at the time of the oath become specified? From this type, if one endows a town for the Haram al-Sharif and stipulates supervision for the qadi, does it refer to the qadi of the Haram, the qadi of the endowed town, or the qadi of the founder's town? It should be derived from the issue of an orphan in one town with property in another: does the supervision belong to the qadi of the orphan's town or the qadi of the property's town? They explicitly ruled for the first, so presumably supervision should be for the qadi of...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage examines the role of custom in acknowledgment (iqrar), lawsuits (da'wa), and endowments (waqf), contrasting them with contracts. The author begins by stating that iqrar is an informing about a past obligation, which may precede the dominant custom. Therefore, if someone acknowledges dirhams and later specifies they were counterfeit, he is believed if he connects (wasala) the specification to the acknowledgment. However, for a specific sum like a thousand from the price of goods or a loan, Imam Abu Hanifa does not believe him whether he connects or not, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) believe him if he connects. For usurpation or deposit, he is believed absolutely. Lawsuits, like iqrar, report past events and are not interpreted according to current custom. The author quotes al-Bazzaziyya from al-Lamishi: if multiple currencies exist and one is more current, a lawsuit is not valid without specifying which currency; similarly, acknowledging ten red dinars is valid without further specification even if other red currencies exist, because the word 'red' suffices. In contrast, a sale is interpreted according to the most current currency. Then two derived issues: (1) School stipends (batata): if a custom has been established for certain months, it applies to endowments made after that custom, not before. (2) Waqf supervision: if the founder stipulates supervision for the current qadi, and later the qadi's school changes (e.g., from Shafi'i to Hanafi), the later qadi does not automatically inherit supervision because the stipulation was for that specific qadi. The author notes an oath analogy: if a ruler makes someone swear to inform him of troublemakers, the oath becomes void upon the ruler's dismissal. Finally, unresolved questions: if someone swears to report wrongdoing to a qadi, which qadi is intended? And if a town is endowed for the Haram and supervision is for the qadi, which qadi (of Haram, endowed town, or founder's town) is meant? The author suggests analogy with orphan's property: the supervisor is the qadi of the orphan's town.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): A statement by which a person informs of a right or obligation due from him to another
البطالة (al-batata): Stipends or salaries given to students or teachers in schools, often distributed in specific months

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both are equally interpreted by current custom
B) Contract is interpreted by custom; acknowledgment is not because it reports a past obligation
C) Acknowledgment is interpreted by custom; contract is not
D) Neither is interpreted by custom

Key Takeaway
Lawsuit and acknowledgment are reports of the past and thus not interpreted by later custom; contracts are initiated immediately and are subject to custom.

General vs Specific Custom in Hanafi Law
Analyzing whether only general custom or also specific custom establishes rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between general custom ('urf 'amm) and specific custom ('urf khas) to evaluate whether a given custom can establish a ruling.

Core Idea
Only general custom establishes rulings; specific custom may be applied by fatwa when necessary.

The maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative) is a cornerstone of Hanafi fatwa, but not every custom qualifies. The school distinguishes between general custom ('urf 'amm), which is widespread among all people, and specific custom ('urf khas), limited to a locality or group. The dominant Hanafi position holds that only general custom can establish a binding ruling. However, later scholars often applied specific custom in practice due to necessity or widespread acceptance. This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir explores this tension and its practical implications, including famous cases like khulu al-hawanit (key money for shops) in Cairo.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custom and its scope
Determine whether the custom is general ('amm) or specific (khas) by assessing its prevalence across regions and communities. A custom limited to a single town, profession, or elite group is specific.
The passage gives examples: custom of Bukhara's elite, custom of a single town altering weights, custom of Cairote fuqaha.
2
Apply the dominant rule
Recall that the madhhab (dominant school position) is: 'al-hukm al-'amm la yathbutu bil-'urf al-khas' — a general ruling is not established by specific custom. Only general custom is authoritative for deriving rulings.
There is an alternative view that specific custom can establish rulings, but it is considered weak (marjuh) in the school.
3
Check for textual conflict
If the custom contradicts a clear text (nass) from Quran or Sunnah, it is invalid regardless of generality. The maxim 'al-nusus muqaddamah 'ala al-'urf' (texts take precedence over custom) applies.
This istithna' is crucial; custom never overrides a definitive text.
4
Evaluate necessity and scholarly practice
Examine if later Hanafi mashayikh have applied a specific custom due to need (hajah) or widespread practice among Muslims. The passage notes that many scholars gave fatwa considering specific custom, e.g., bai' al-wafa, hiring weavers, and Cairo practices.
This reflects a pragmatic approach: when general custom is silent and specific custom is widely accepted without textual violation, fatwa may adopt it.
5
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
For fatwa, the dominant position (mufta bihi) is the madhhab: general custom only. However, the secondary position (applying specific custom) may be used when there is need and it does not conflict with a stronger proof. The mufti must weigh the evidence.
The author, Ibn Nujaym, leans toward applying specific custom for Cairo cases like khulu al-hawanit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیه
·
هل يعتبر في بناء الأحكام العُرْف العام أو مطلق العُرْف ولو كان خاصا؟ المذهب الأول. قال في البزازية معزيّا إلى الإمام البخاري الذي ختم به الفقه : الحكم العام لا يثبت بالعُرْف الخاص وقيل يثبت. (انتهى).
ويتفرع على ذلك لو استقرض ألفًا واستأجر المقرض لحفظ مرآة أو ملعقة كل شهر بعشرة وقيمتها لا تزيد على الأخر ففيها ثلاثة أقوال صحة الإجارة بلا كراهة اعتبارًا لعُزف خواص بخارى والصحة مع الكراهة للاختلاف، والفساد لأن صحة الإجارة بالتعارف العام ولم يوجد وقد أفتى الأكابر بفسادها. وفي القنية من باب استئجار المستقرض المقرض : التعارف الذي تثبت به الأحكام لا يثبت بتعارف أهل بلدة واحدة عند البعض. وعند البعض إن كان يثبت ولكن أحدثه بعض أهل بخارى فلم يكن متعارفًا مطلقا كيف وأن هذا الشيء لم يعرفه عامتهم بل تعارفه خواصهم فلا يثبت التعارف بهذا القدر، قال رضي الله عنه : وهو الصواب. (انتهى).
وذكر فيها من كتاب الكراهية قبيل التحرّي ؛ لو تواضع أهل بلدة على زيادة في سنجاتهم التي توزن بها الدراهم والإبريسم (۱) على مخالفة سائر البلدان ليس لهم ذلك . (انتهى). وفي إجارة البزازية في إجارة الأصل؛ استأجره ليحمل طعامه بقفيز منه فالإجارة فاسدة ويجب أجر المثل لا يتجاور به المسمى، وكذا إذا دفع إلى حائك غزلاً على أن ينسجه بالثلث. ومشايخ بلخ وخوارزم أفتوا بجواز إجارة الحائك للعزف وبه أفتى أبو علي النسفي أيضًا الفتوى على جواب الكتاب لا الطحان لأنه منصوص عليه فيلزم إبطال النص. (انتهى).
أنه وفيها من البيع الفاسد في الكلام على بيع الوفاء في القول السادس من صحيح قالوا لحاجة الناس إليه فرارًا من الربا؛ فأهل بلخ اعتادوا الدين والإجارة وهي
لا تصح في الكرم وأهل بخارى اعتادوا الإجارة الطويلة ولا يمكن في الأشجار فاضطروا إلى بيعها وفاء وما ضاق على الناس أمر إلا اتَّسع حكمه . (انتهى) . والحاصل أن المذهب عدم اعتبار العُرْف الخاص، ولكن أفتى كثير من المشايخ باعتباره ؛ فأقول على اعتباره ينبغي أن يفتى بأن ما يقع في بعض أسواق القاهرة من خلو الحوانيت لازم ويصير الخلوّ في الحانوت حقًا له ؛ فلا يملك صاحب الحانوت إخراجه منها ولا إجارتها لغيره ولو كانت وقفًا. وقد وقع في حوانيت الجملون بالغورية أن السلطان الغوري لما بناها أسكنها للتجار بالخلو وجعل لكل حانوت قدرًا أخذه منهم وكتب ذلك بمكتوب الوقف وكذا أقول على اعتبار العُرْف الخاص، قد تعارف الفقهاء بالقاهرة النزول عن الوظائف بمال يُعطى لصاحبها وتعارفوا ذلك فينبغي الجواز، وإنه لو نزل له وقبض منه المبلغ ثم أراد الرجوع عليه لا يملك ذلك. ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم. وقد اعتبروا عُزف القاهرة في مسائل ؛ منها ما في فتح القدير من دخول السلم في البيت المبيع في القاهرة دون غيرها لأن بيوتهم طبقات لا ينتفع بها إلا به .
Attention: Is the general custom considered in building rulings, or absolute custom even if specific? The first school (of thought). He said in al-Bazaziyya, attributing to al-Imam al-Bukhari, the one with whom fiqh was sealed: The general ruling is not established by specific custom. And it was said it is established. (End). And branching from that: if one borrowed a thousand and the lender hired him to preserve a mirror or a spoon every month for ten, and its value does not exceed the other, then concerning it there are three sayings: validity of the hire without dislike considering the custom of the elite of Bukhara; validity with dislike due to disagreement; and invalidity because the validity of hire is by general custom, which does not exist. And the senior scholars gave a fatwa for its invalidity. In al-Qunyah from the chapter of the borrower hiring the lender: the customary practice by which rulings are established is not established by the custom of a single town according to some. And according to some, it is established, but some people of Bukhara innovated it, so it was not universally customary. How so? This thing was not known by their general public, rather their elite customarily practiced it, so custom is not established by this amount. He (may Allah be pleased with him) said: that is correct. (End). And he mentioned in it from the Book of Disliked Acts near the section of taharri: If the people of a town agree on an increase in their weights by which dirhams and silk are weighed, contrary to all other towns, they have no right to that. (End). In the Ijarah of al-Bazaziyya in the hire of the original: He hired him to carry his food for a qafiz of it, so the hire is void and the fair wage is obligatory, not exceeding the named (amount). Likewise, if he gave a weaver yarn to weave for a third. And the mashayikh of Balkh and Khwarizm gave a fatwa for the permissibility of hiring the weaver for the custom, and Abu Ali al-Nasafi also gave a fatwa according to the fatwa of the book, not the miller, because it is textually stated, so it necessitates nullifying the text. (End). And in it, from the chapter of invalid sale in the discussion on bai' al-wafa in the sixth saying of Sahih, they said due to people's need for it to flee from riba: so the people of Balkh practiced debt and hire, and it is not valid for vines, and the people of Bukhara practiced long-term hire, and it is not possible in the case of trees, so they were forced to bai' al-wafa. And no affair became constrained for people but its ruling expanded. (End). The conclusion is that the school is the non-consideration of specific custom, but many mashayikh gave fatwa for its consideration. So I say: on its consideration, it should be given fatwa that what occurs in some markets of Cairo of the khulu (key money) of shops is binding, and the khulu in the shop becomes a right for him; so the owner of the shop cannot remove him from it nor rent it to another, even if it is a waqf. And it occurred in the shops of al-Jamalun in al-Ghuriyya that Sultan al-Ghuri when he built them, he housed the merchants by khulu and set for each shop an amount he took from them, and wrote that in the waqf document. Likewise, I say on the consideration of specific custom: the fuqaha in Cairo have customarily practiced relinquishing positions (nuzul an al-waza'if) for money given to their holder, and they customarily practiced that, so permissibility should be followed, and if he relinquished and took the amount from him and then wanted to retract, he does not have that right. And there is no power and no strength except by Allah the Most High, the Great. And they considered the custom of Cairo in issues: among them is what is in Fath al-Qadir of the stairs entering into the sold house in Cairo unlike elsewhere, because their houses are multi-story and cannot be benefited from except by that.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage addresses a key question in the application of the maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative). The Hanafi school distinguishes between general custom ('urf 'amm) which is widespread among all people, and specific custom ('urf khas) which is limited to a particular locality or group. The dominant position (madhhab) is that only general custom can establish a ruling. The text begins by stating this clearly: 'al-madhhab al-awwal' (the first school) is that only general custom is considered. It then cites al-Bazaziyya: 'al-hukm al-'amm la yathbutu bil-'urf al-khas' (the general ruling is not established by specific custom). However, there is a saying that it is established. This internal ikhtilaf is important. The furuat (applications) follow: the case of a lender hiring the borrower to preserve items—three opinions arise based on whether the custom of Bukhara's elite is considered. The senior scholars (akabir) preferred invalidity, showing caution. Al-Quniya adds that the custom of a single town is insufficient to establish rulings unless it becomes universal. The example of altering weights is a clear violation of general custom. The text then moves to more complex cases: hiring a weaver, bai' al-wafa, and finally the contemporary (for the author) practice of khulu al-hawanit (key money for shops) in Cairo. The author, after noting that many mashayikh did consider specific custom, applies this to Cairo: the custom of paying a fee for shop tenancy (khulu) becomes binding, and the owner cannot evict the tenant even if it is a waqf. Similarly, the practice of 'nuzul an al-waza'if' (relinquishing positions for money) among Cairo's fuqaha is permissible by this custom. The passage ends by noting another example from Fath al-Qadir: in Cairo, the stairs of a house are included in the sale because custom dictates it. This demonstrates how a specific custom can create a legal effect when relied upon. The overall lesson: while the formal madhhab rejects specific custom, practical fatwa (especially in the later tradition) often accommodates it due to need and custom of the people.

Key Terms
عرف عام (urf 'amm): Custom widespread among all people, not limited to a locality or profession.
عرف خاص (urf khas): Custom limited to a specific town, profession, or group.
خلو الحوانيت (khulu al-hawanit): Key money or tenancy right in shops, a practice where a tenant pays a lump sum for the right to occupy and can then transfer that right.
التعارف (al-ta'aruf): Mutual customary practice among people.
النزول عن الوظائف (al-nuzul 'an al-waza'if): Relinquishing positions (e.g., teaching posts) for money, a customary practice among fuqaha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the dominant Hanafi position regarding specific custom ('urf khas)?
A) It is authoritative in all matters.
B) It is not authoritative for establishing general rulings, but many later scholars applied it.
C) It is only authoritative in financial transactions.
D) It is authoritative only if it contradicts the Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that only general custom establishes rulings, but specific custom may be applied by fatwa when there is need and it does not contradict a clear text.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications

The Maxim: Ijtihad Is Not Overturned by Ijtihad
Qawida with its proof, illa, and multiple furuat from fiqh chapters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ijtihad la yunqad bi al-ijtihad' to identify when a past ruling remains binding despite a change in ijtihad, and evaluate the exceptions where the maxim does not apply.

Core Idea
A past ijtihad-based ruling is not overturned by a later ijtihad; the first remains binding to preserve stability and avoid hardship.

This lesson introduces one of the most important maxims of Hanafi legal procedure: once a ruling is made by ijtihad, a later ijtihad does not undo it. We will examine its proof from the practice of the Companions, its underlying wisdom, and the concrete cases where it applies—including its exceptions. Understanding this maxim is crucial for muftis and judges to ensure consistency and finality in legal decisions.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Read the maxim in Arabic and English. Note that it is a wusta (intermediate) kulliya (universal) rule used primarily in qada and fatwa.
Memorise: الاجتهاد لا ينقض بالاجتهاد
2
Identify the dalil and illa
The proof is ijma (consensus) from the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar. The illa is that the second ijtihad is not stronger than the first, and overturning would lead to instability and hardship.
The author critiques al-Hidayah's alternate illa: 'the first is strengthened by being connected to judgment'.
3
Work through the furu' on qibla
If a person changes ijtihad about the qibla during prayer, they follow the second. If already prayed four rak'ats to different directions by ijtihad, no qada. Understand the scenario of returning to the first direction.
Note the ikhtilaf in al-Khulasa about repeating the rak'ah.
4
Analyze the testimony and garment/vessel cases
A rejected fasiq's testimony after repentance is not re-accepted in the same case (except four exceptions). Similarly, if someone uses one garment thinking it pure, then changes ijtihad, the second ijtihad is ignored; for two vessels, tayammum is done.
Both cases illustrate that the executed ijtihad stands.
5
Examine the judge's own change of ijtihad and enforcement of others' judgments
A judge does not overturn his past judgments but applies new ijtihad to future cases. He must enforce another judge's ijtihadi judgment if it does not contradict clear texts and ijma.
This is the meaning of 'imda' in the book of Qada.
6
Evaluate the exceptions
The maxim has exceptions. One is division (qisma) where gross exploitation (ghubn fahish) appears: the division is overturned because the condition of equity was absent from the start. The second exception is incomplete in the text.
The division is overturned not by new ijtihad but because the original lacked a condition, so it was invalid ab initio.

النوع الثاني من القواعد
القاعدة الأولى : الاجتهاد لا ينقض بالاجتهاد
ودليلها الإجماع وقد حكم أبو بكر رضي الله عنه في مسائل وخالفه عمر رضي الله عنه فيها، ولم ينقض حكمه وعلته بأنه ليس الاجتهاد الثاني بأقوى من الأول وأنه يؤدي إلى أن لا يستقر حكم وفيه مشقة شديدة وهذا أولى من قوله في الهداية : لأن الاجتهاد الثاني كالاجتهاد الأول وقد ترجح الأول باتصال القضاء به فلا ينقض لما هو دونه (انتهى). لأنه يكفي بأن الثاني كالأول ولا حاجة إلى ترجيح الأول بغير السبق ما أورده في العناية على قوله إن الأول ترجح باتصال القضاء بأنه ترجيح للأصل بفرعه، لأن الأصل في القضاء رأي المجتهد فكيف يترجح بالقضاء. وإن أجاب عنه
أصله بأن الفرع يرجح من حيث بقاؤه لا من حيث إنه منه فالشيئان إذا تساويا في القوة وكان لأحدهما فرع فإنه يترجح على ما لا فرع له إلى آخره. ومن فروع ذلك لو تغير اجتهاده في القبلة عمل بالثاني حتى لو صلّى أربع ركعات إلى أربع جهات بالاجتهاد فلا قضاء. وإنما اختلفوا فيما لو صلى ركعة بالتحري إلى جهة ثم تغيَّر إلى أخرى ثم عاد إلى الأولى. وقد بيناه في الشرح، وذكر فيه اختلافا في الخلاصة، منهم من قال لا يستقبل ومنهم من قال يستقبل. (انتهى).
ومنها لو حكم القاضي بردّ شهادة الفاسق ثم تاب فأعادها لم تقبل. وعلله بعضهم بأن قبول شهادته بعد التوبة يتضمن نقض الاجتهاد بالاجتهاد. وأصله كما في الخلاصة : مَن ردّت شهادته لعلَّة ثم زالت ثم أعادها في تلك الحادثة لم تُقبل إلا في أربعة الصبي والعبد ،والكافر، والأعمى. (انتهى).
ومنها لو كان لرجل ثوبان أحدهما نجس، فتحرّى بأحدهما وصلى ثم وقع تحريه على طهارة الآخر لم يعتبر الثاني، وعلى هذا مسألة في الشهادات: شهدت طائفة بقتله يوم النحر بمكة وطائفة بموته يومه بالكوفة ، لغتا(۱): فإن قضى بأحديهما قبل حضور الأخرى لم تعتبر الثانية لاتصال القضاء بها ومقتضى الأول أنه لو تحرّى وظن طهارة أحد الإنائين فاستعمله وترك الآخر ثم تغيّر ظنه لا يعمل بالثاني بل يتيمم، مبني على جواز التحري في الإنائين. وفي شرح المجمع قبيل التيمم : لو كانا إنائين . يريقهما ويتيمم اتفاقا . (انتهى).
ولكن هذا
ومنها لو حكم الحاكم بشيء، ثم تغيَّر اجتهاده لا ينقض الأول ويحكم بالمستقبل بما رآه ثانيا .

And now we begin the second type of rules: universal rules from which countless particular instances are derived. The second type of rules. The first rule: Ijtihad is not overturned by ijtihad. Its proof is consensus (ijma), and Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, judged in matters and Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, disagreed with him in them, but did not overturn his ruling. The cause (illa) is that the second ijtihad is not stronger than the first, and it leads to no ruling becoming settled, and there is severe hardship in it. This is better than his statement in al-Hidayah: 'Because the second ijtihad is like the first, and the first is strengthened by being connected to the judgment, so it is not overturned by what is weaker than it' (end). Because it is sufficient that the second is like the first, and there is no need to give preponderance to the first without precedent, as mentioned in al-Inayah regarding his statement that the first is strengthened by being connected to judgment – that it is giving preponderance to the original through its branch, because the basis of judgment is the mujtahid's opinion, so how can it be strengthened by judgment? And he responded about it from its source: that the branch is strengthened by its remaining, not by being from it; so when two things are equal in strength and one of them has a branch, it is given preponderance over the one without a branch, etc. Among its applications: if his ijtihad changes regarding the qibla, he acts by the second, even if he prayed four rak'ats to four directions by ijtihad, there is no qada. But they differed when he prayed one rak'ah by ijtihad towards a direction, then changed to another, then returned to the first. And we explained it in the commentary, and mentioned a difference in al-Khulasa: some said he does not repeat, some said he repeats. (End). Among them: if a judge rejected the testimony of a corrupt person (fasiq), then he repented and re-presented it, it is not accepted. Some have justified it that accepting his testimony after repentance implies overturning ijtihad by ijtihad. Its source as in al-Khulasa: someone whose testimony was rejected for a cause, then the cause ceased, then he re-presented it in that same incident, it is not accepted except in four: the boy, the slave, the disbeliever, and the blind. (End). Among them: if a man has two garments, one of them impure, he made ijtihad and used one and prayed, then his ijtihad fell on the purity of the other, the second is not considered. And on this basis is a case in testimony: a group testified that he was killed on the day of Nahr in Makkah, and another group testified that he died on the same day in Kufa – they are nullified. If he gave judgment based on one before the other's presence, the second is not considered because of the judgment being connected to it. And the implication of the first is that if he made ijtihad and thought one of two vessels was pure, so he used it and left the other, then his opinion changed, he does not act by the second, but rather performs tayammum, based on the permissibility of ijtihad regarding vessels. And in Sharh al-Majma' before Tayammum: if there are two vessels, he pours them out and does tayammum, unanimously. (End). But this... Among them: if a ruler judged something, then his ijtihad changed, he does not overturn the first, and he judges in the future by what he sees as correct secondly. Among them: the judgment of a judge in ijtihadi matters is not overturned, and that is the meaning of our companions' statement in the book of Qada: if a judge's judgment is presented to him, he enforces it as long as it does not contradict the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma. And we have explained the conditions of judgment and the meaning of enforcement in Sharh al-Kanz, and we have written the exceptional issues in the second type. Then know that some have excepted from this rule, namely 'ijtihad is not overturned by ijtihad', two issues: one of them – overturning a division (qisma) if gross exploitation (ghubn fahish) appears in it; it occurred by ijtihad, so how is it overturned by the like? And the answer is that its overturning is due to the failure of its condition from the beginning, which is equity, so it appears that... (1) Meaning: they are nullified; and in al-Misbah: lagha al-shay' yalghu: it is invalid.

Commentary
This passage is an extensive treatment of the qawida 'Ijtihad is not overturned by ijtihad', which is the first rule in the second type of qawaid. The author provides its evidence (ijma, citing the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar), its legal cause (illa), and engages in a critical discussion of the wording in al-Hidayah and al-Inayah. Then, several furuat (applications) are given: (1) change in qibla direction during prayer – the second ijtihad is followed, except for a specific scenario where scholars differ; (2) a judge rejecting testimony of a fasiq who later repents – the testimony is not accepted in the same case because it would overturn the previous ijtihad, except for four types of witnesses; (3) ijtihad in choosing between two garments/vessels – if one's ijtihad changes after using one, the second is not considered, and in case of two equal vessels, one performs tayammum; (4) a judge's own change of ijtihad does not overturn past judgments but applies to future cases; and (5) judgments of other judges in ijtihadi matters must be enforced if they do not violate clear texts. Finally, the author mentions two exceptions: (a) division of property (qisma) where gross exploitation appears – the division is overturned because the condition of equity was absent from the start; (b) the text cuts off before stating the second exception. This passage shows the richness of Hanafi usul al-iftai in applying a single maxim to diverse fiqh issues.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The effort of a qualified scholar (mujtahid) to derive a ruling from the sources when no clear text exists.
القضاء (al-qada): Legal judgment passed by a judge or ruler, often binding on the parties.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fahish): Gross exploitation or unfairness in a transaction, typically more than is normally tolerated by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad does not overturn a previous ijtihad that has been acted upon; this preserves legal stability and avoids hardship.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications

Ijtihad is Not Invalidated by Ijtihad: The Rule and Its Applications
Explaining the qawida with its basis, applications, and two exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ijtihad la yunqad bi al-ijtihad' to furu' from qada, shahadat, and taharah, and distinguish the two apparent exceptions that do not actually break the rule.

Core Idea
A valid ijtihad-based ruling stands even if later ijtihad differs – stability in legal continuity.

This lesson unpacks one of the most important legal maxims for muftis and judges: 'Ijtihad is not invalidated by ijtihad.' The maxim ensures that rulings based on careful reasoning are not overturned simply because the same jurist or another jurist later arrives at a different ijtihad. Without this principle, judicial and fatwa systems would lack finality and trust. We will explore the rational basis of the rule, walk through concrete applications from Salah, Shahadat, Taharah, and Qada, and then examine two cases that appear to be exceptions but actually confirm the rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule and its basis
The maxim states that a ruling derived by ijtihad is not invalidated by a subsequent ijtihad from the same or another jurist. The basis is that when two ijtihads are equal in strength, the one that has generated a legal effect (a subsidiary ruling or action already taken) is stronger due to its continuous effect. This is like preferring the existing over the potential.
The source text explains: 'الفرع يرجح من حيث بقاؤه' – the subsidiary is stronger because of its continuity.
2
Apply the rule to furu' from different chapters
The text gives five applications: (1) changing ijtihad on qibla after praying – prayer valid; (2) rejecting a repentant sinner's testimony in the same case; (3) disregarding a later taharri after already performing prayer; (4) a judge's own change of ijtihad does not void past rulings; (5) a later judge must enforce a previous judge's ijtihad-based ruling unless it contradicts nass/ijma. Work through each to see how the rule operates.
Pay attention to the exception within the qibla case: if one prays only one rak'ah then changes, there is ikhtilaf – some say he must face the new direction.
3
Analyze the two apparent exceptions and why they do not break the rule
The text mentions two cases that seem to violate the maxim: (1) invalidating a division (qismah) if gross disparity appears, and (2) a new imam changing the predecessor's public policies. In both, the original act was not truly based on valid ijtihad because it lacked a condition (equality in division) or was based on public interest (which changes with circumstances). Thus, the maxim remains intact.
The key is that the initial action was invalid ab initio due to missing condition (qismah) or was never a fixed ijtihad but a policy (imam).
4
Distinguish between invalidating the initial ruling and changing ijtihad
Invalidating a ruling for lack of condition (like equality in division) is not 'invalidating ijtihad by ijtihad' – it is recognizing that the original was never a valid ijtihad. True ijtihad requires all conditions to be met. If a condition was missing, the ruling is void from the start, and this is not a change of ijtihad but a correction of error.
This distinction is crucial for muftis: when a condition is discovered to be absent, the ruling is void regardless of ijtihad.
5
Apply the maxim to modern fatwa practice
As a mufti, you may issue a fatwa based on ijtihad on a new issue (e.g., cryptocurrency). Later, after further research, your ijtihad changes. According to this maxim, you do not retroactively invalidate those who acted on your first fatwa. You should issue the new fatwa for future cases. This ensures trust in the fatwa system.
The maxim applies equally to judges (qada) and muftis, though the fatwa is advisory, not binding like a judgment.

أصله بأن الفرع يرجح من حيث بقاؤه لا من حيث إنه منه فالشيئان إذا تساويا في القوة وكان لأحدهما فرع فإنه يترجح على ما لا فرع له إلى آخره. ومن فروع ذلك لو تغير اجتهاده في القبلة عمل بالثاني حتى لو صلّى أربع ركعات إلى أربع جهات بالاجتهاد فلا قضاء. وإنما اختلفوا فيما لو صلى ركعة بالتحري إلى جهة ثم تغيَّر إلى أخرى ثم عاد إلى الأولى. وقد بيناه في الشرح، وذكر فيه اختلافا في الخلاصة، منهم من قال لا يستقبل ومنهم من قال يستقبل. (انتهى). ومنها لو حكم القاضي بردّ شهادة الفاسق ثم تاب فأعادها لم تقبل. وعلله بعضهم بأن قبول شهادته بعد التوبة يتضمن نقض الاجتهاد بالاجتهاد. وأصله كما في الخلاصة : مَن ردّت شهادته لعلَّة ثم زالت ثم أعادها في تلك الحادثة لم تُقبل إلا في أربعة الصبي والعبد ،والكافر، والأعمى. (انتهى). ومنها لو كان لرجل ثوبان أحدهما نجس، فتحرّى بأحدهما وصلى ثم وقع تحريه على طهارة الآخر لم يعتبر الثاني، وعلى هذا مسألة في الشهادات: شهدت طائفة بقتله يوم النحر بمكة وطائفة بموته يومه بالكوفة ، لغتا: فإن قضى بأحديهما قبل حضور الأخرى لم تعتبر الثانية لاتصال القضاء بها ومقتضى الأول أنه لو تحرّى وظن طهارة أحد الإنائين فاستعمله وترك الآخر ثم تغيّر ظنه لا يعمل بالثاني بل يتيمم، مبني على جواز التحري في الإنائين. وفي شرح المجمع قبيل التيمم : لو كانا إنائين . يريقهما ويتيمم اتفاقا . (انتهى). ومنها لو حكم الحاكم بشيء، ثم تغيَّر اجتهاده لا ينقض الأول ويحكم بالمستقبل بما رآه ثانيا . ومنها حكم القاضي في المسائل الاجتهادية لا ينقض وهو معنى قول أصحابنا في كتاب القضاء : إذا رفع إليه حكم حاكم أمضاه إن لم يخالف الكتاب والسنة والإجماع. وقد بيّنا شروط القضاء ومعنى الإمضاء في شرح الكنز وكتبنا المسائل المستثناة في النوع الثاني . ثم اعلم أن بعضهم استثنى من هذه القاعدة أعني الاجتهاد لا ينقض بالاجتهاد مسألتين : إحداهما نقض القسمة إذا ظهر فيها غبن فاحش فإنها وقعت بالاجتهاد فكيف ينقض بمثله؟ والجواب أن نقضها لفوات شرطها في الابتداء، وهو المعادلة فظهر أنها لم تكن صحيحة من الابتداء، فهو كما لو ظهر خطأ القاضي بفوات شرط فإنه ينقض قضاؤه، والثانية : إذا رأى الإمام شيئًا ثم مات أو عزل فللثاني تغييره حيث كان من الأمور العامة والجواب أن هذا حكم يدور مع المصلحة، فإذا رآها الثاني وجب اتباعها .
Its basis: the subsidiary is considered stronger because of its continuity, not because it originates from the original. So two things when equal in strength, one having a subsidiary, it is preferred over that without a subsidiary, etc. Among its applications: If his ijtihad regarding the qibla changes, he acts on the second [opinion]; even if he prayed four rak'ahs to four directions by ijtihad, no make-up is required. They differed about one who prayed one rak'ah by taharri to one direction, then changed to another, then returned to the first: we have explained it in the commentary, and an ikhtilaf is mentioned in al-Khulasa: some say he does not face [the first] again, others say he does. (End). And among them: If a judge rejected the testimony of a sinner, then he repented and re-offered it, it is not accepted. Some explained it: accepting his testimony after repentance entails overturning ijtihad by ijtihad. Its origin in al-Khulasa: Whose testimony was rejected for a cause, then the cause ceased, then he re-offered it in the same incident, it is not accepted except in four: the child, the slave, the disbeliever, and the blind. (End). And among them: If a man has two garments, one of which is impure, he performs taharri with one and prays, then his taharri falls on the purity of the other, the second is not regarded. Based on this is a matter in testimony: a group testified that he was killed on the Day of Nahr in Makkah, and another group that he died the same day in Kufa, both are invalid: if he judged by one before the other appeared, the second is not considered due to the connection of the judgment to it. The implication of the first is that if he performed taharri and thought one of two vessels pure, used it and left the other, then his opinion changed, he does not act on the second but rather performs tayammum – based on the permissibility of taharri in two vessels. In Sharh al-Majma' before tayammum: if there are two vessels, he empties them and performs tayammum by agreement. (End). And among them: If a judge rules on something, then his ijtihad changes, he does not invalidate the first and judges the future according to what he now sees. And among them: The judgment of a judge in ijtihad matters is not invalidated – this is the meaning of our companions' statement in Kitab al-Qada: If a previous judge's ruling is brought to him, he upholds it unless it contradicts the Book, Sunnah, or consensus. We have explained the conditions of qada and the meaning of enforcement in Sharh al-Kanz and wrote the exceptional cases in the second type. Then know that some excepted from this rule, i.e., ijtihad is not invalidated by ijtihad, two matters: First: invalidating the division if a gross disadvantage appears in it – because it occurred by ijtihad, so how is it invalidated by the like? The answer: its invalidation is due to the initial loss of a condition, which is equality; thus it appears it was not valid from the beginning, like if the judge's error appears due to a missing condition, his judgment is invalidated. The second: If the imam sees something, then dies or is removed, the second imam may change it if it is among public matters. The answer: this ruling revolves around public interest; if the second sees it, he must follow it.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage expands the foundational maxim 'Ijtihad is not invalidated by ijtihad' by providing its basis in legal theory and illustrating it with several applications (furu'at) from different chapters of fiqh. The author explains that the principle rests on the idea that when two equivalent pieces of evidence exist, the one that has a subsidiary (i.e., a ruling built upon it) is stronger due to its established effect. The applications include: (1) changing opinion on qibla direction – once a prayer is performed based on ijtihad, it is not repeated even if ijtihad changes, though there is a slight difference if one prays a single rak'ah then changes; (2) rejecting the re-offered testimony of a repentant sinner – because it would overturn the initial ijtihad; (3) taharri in cases of impurity – once a judgment is made and acted upon, a contrary later opinion is disregarded; (4) a judge's change of ijtihad does not void past rulings, only future ones; (5) a later judge must enforce a previous judge's ruling in ijtihad matters unless it violates a clear text. The passage then addresses two apparent exceptions: (a) overturning a division (qismah) if gross disparity appears – but this is not a true exception because the original division was invalid from the start due to missing the condition of equality; (b) a new imam may change the predecessor's public policies – because these rulings are tied to public interest and are not fixed by ijtihad in the same way. Thus, the maxim remains intact.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Legal reasoning by a qualified jurist or judge to derive a ruling in absence of clear text
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fahish): A gross disproportion in value that undermines the validity of a division

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A judge rules based on ijtihad that a certain testimony is acceptable. Later, his ijtihad changes and he believes it is actually unacceptable. According to the rule 'الاجتهاد لا ينقض بالاجتهاد', what is the effect on his earlier ruling?
A) The earlier ruling is invalidated and must be reversed.
B) The earlier ruling stands and is not reversed.
C) The judge must re-open the case and issue a new ruling.
D) The judge must refer the case to another judge.

Key Takeaway
The rule 'al-ijtihad la yunqad bi al-ijtihad' ensures legal finality; its two exceptions confirm rather than break the principle.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
