Wine-Fed Sheep: Istihsan Overrides Strict Qiyas
Application of ghālib principle to meat of wine-fed sheep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to cases where an animal consumes a forbidden substance and explain why immediate slaughter differs from delayed slaughter.

Core Idea
Immediate slaughter after wine-drinking keeps meat halal; delay makes it makruh.

This passage from al-Bazzaziyya illustrates how the Hanafi school uses istihsan to override strict qiyas. The general principle based on impurity would forbid the meat, but the fuqaha' rule that immediate slaughter prevents the wine from affecting the flesh. This is a furu' of the broader qa'ida 'al-ghalib' and an application of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Find the explicit ruling for immediate slaughter: the meat is halal without dislike.
2
Note the strict rule
The strict qiyas would prohibit the meat because the animal consumed a najis substance. Understand why this is the default position.
This is the 'muqtada al-qa'ida' in the text.
3
Understand the istihsan
The Hanafi jurists apply istihsan to permit the meat if slaughtered immediately, considering that the wine has not yet penetrated the flesh.
Analogous to feeding haram fodder – milk and meat remain halal, though caution is recommended.
4
Distinguish immediate vs delayed
Note that if slaughter occurs after an hour or a day, the meat is halal but with dislike (karaha). This is because the wine may have begun to affect the flesh.
The ruling changes due to the passage of time, reflecting the ghālib principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : لو سقى شاة خمرًا ثم ذبحها من ساعته فإنها تحلّ بلا كراهة كذا في البزازية ومقتضى القاعدة التحريم، ومقتضى الفرع أنه لو علفها علفا حراما، لم يحرم لبنها ولحمها وإن كان الورع الترك، ثم قال في البزازية بعده ولو بعد ساعة إلى يوم تحلّ مع الكراهة. (انتهى).
The fourth: If one gives a sheep wine to drink and then slaughters it immediately, it is permissible without dislike. Thus in al-Bazzāziyya. The [general] rule [based on impurity] would require prohibition, but the branch ruling [from the fuqahā'] is that if one feeds it forbidden fodder, its milk and meat do not become unlawful, though caution is to leave it. Then he said in al-Bazzāziyya after it: And if [slaughtered] after an hour or a day, it is permissible with dislike. End.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an application of the principle of istihsān overriding strict qiyās. The strict qiyās (based on impurity of wine) would deem the meat forbidden, but the Ḥanafī fuqahā' rule that immediate slaughter prevents the wine from affecting the meat. Interestingly, the text notes that feeding a sheep haram fodder does not make its meat or milk haram, though it is recommended to avoid. The ruling distinguishes between immediate slaughter (no dislike) and slaughter after some time (makrūh), likely because after a delay the effects may have penetrated the flesh. This is a furū' of the broader qā'ida 'al-ghālib' but more specifically istihsān. The source is al-Bazzāziyya, a later fatāwā collection.

Key Terms
حلّ (ḥalla): Legally permissible to eat
كراهة (karāha): Dislike, i.e., makrūh (closer to ḥarām in some cases)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a sheep is given wine and slaughtered immediately, the meat is:
A) Haram
B) Halal without dislike
C) Halal but makrūh
D) Makrūh tanzīhan

Key Takeaway
Immediate slaughter after wine-drinking keeps meat halal; delay introduces dislike (karaha) due to potential penetration.

Istihlāk and the Muhrim's Perfume
Application of istihlāk to perfume in muhrim's food

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qāʻida of istihlāk to determine fidyah liability for a muhrim consuming food containing perfume.

Core Idea
When a prohibited substance is completely overwhelmed, the prohibition lifts and no fidyah is due.

This passage illustrates a key furūʻ under the broader qāʻida 'al-ghālib yulḥaq' (the overwhelming attribute determines the ruling). Ibn Nujaym applies istihlāk to the case of a muhrim eating food with perfume: if the perfume is mustahlak, there is no penalty. Understanding this application shows how the school softens prohibitions when the prohibited element loses independent identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qāʻida in action
Recognize that the underlying principle is istihlāk – when a forbidden substance is so overwhelmed by a permissible base that it is no longer discernible, its ruling is nullified.
This is a branch of the major qāʻida 'al-ghālib yulḥaq'.
2
Examine the furūʻ (branch case)
The case is a muhrim eating food in which perfume (forbidden during iḥrām) has been completely consumed by the food – i.e., it is not detectable by taste, smell, or sight.
The perfume must be fully mustahlak, not merely mixed.
3
Apply the qāʻida to the furūʻ
Since the perfume is mustahlak, it is as if it does not exist. The act is not considered applying perfume, so the general prohibition on perfume during iḥrām does not apply.
4
State the ruling
The ruling: no fidyah (penalty) is due. The text says 'فلا فدية' (so there is no fidyah).
This ruling is specific to consumption; application of perfume directly still requires fidyah.
5
Identify the scope and limits
The exception only applies when the perfume is truly mustahlak. If even a trace remains discernible, the prohibition holds and fidyah is required. Also, this does not permit intentional addition of perfume to food.
Ibn Nujaym refers to his Sharḥ al-Kanz for further elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : أن يكون الحرام مستهلكا فلو أكل المحرم شيئا قد استهلك فيه الطيب فلا فدية. وقد أوضحناه في شرح الكنز في جنابات الإحرام.
The fifth: That the ḥarām is overwhelmed (mustahlak). For example, if a muhrim eats something in which perfume has been overwhelmed, there is no fidyah. We have explained it in Sharḥ al-Kanz in the section on violations of iḥrām.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage applies the principle that when a prohibited substance (here perfume) is completely consumed/overwhelmed by a permissible base, the prohibition is lifted. For a muhrim, applying perfume is forbidden and requires fidyah. But if the perfume is present only as a trace in food, such that it is no longer discernible, then eating that food does not incur fidyah. The author refers to his own commentary on Kanz al-Daqā'iq for more detail. This is a furū' under the broader qā'ida 'al-ghālib'—the overwhelming permissible substance dominates the ruling.

Key Terms
مستهلك (mustahlak): A prohibited substance that has been completely overwhelmed or nullified by a permissible substance, making it imperceptible.
فدية (fidyah): A compensatory penalty (often an animal sacrifice or equivalent) due for violating iḥrām restrictions.
محرم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of iḥrām for ḥajj or ʿumrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim eats food in which perfume is mustahlak (overwhelmed), what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) He must pay fidyah
B) No fidyah is required
C) He must repeat his iḥrām
D) His iḥrām is invalid

Key Takeaway
The qāʻida of istihlāk removes liability when a prohibited substance is completely overwhelmed, as seen in the case of a muhrim eating perfume in food.

Pure Liquid Mixed with Absolute Water: The Maxim of Predominance
Application of the maxim 'al-ibra li l-ghalib' to water mixed with pure liquid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ibra li l-ghalib' to determine whether a mixture of pure liquid and absolute water can be used for purification.

Core Idea
When a pure liquid mixes with absolute water, the mixture's ruling follows the predominant element.

This lesson presents a direct application of the major Hanafi qawida 'al-ibra li l-ghalib' (consideration is for the predominant). The passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair states the rule concisely and refers to Sharh al-Kanz for detailed guidance on determining predominance. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when asked about the validity of using mixed water for purification.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the qawida from the text: 'العبرة للغالب' (consideration is for the predominant). This is a minor maxim (sughra) that governs mixtures.
2
Identify the case
The case is when a pure liquid (mā'i' ṭāhir) mixes with absolute water (mā' muṭlaq). The term 'pure liquid' means tahir, not najis.
3
Apply the maxim
Determine which element is predominant. If water is the majority, the mixture retains the ruling of water: purification (e.g., wudu) is valid. If the other liquid is the majority, the mixture loses the ruling of water and cannot be used for purification.
4
Determine predominance
The author refers to Sharh al-Kanz for the method of determining 'ghalaba'. Typically, it is determined by volume or by effect on properties (color, taste, smell). However, as long as water is the majority in volume, the mixture remains water in ruling.
Other schools may differ; this is the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة : إذا اختلط مائع طاهر بماء مطلق فالعبرة للغالب، فإن غلب الماء جازت الطهارة به وإلا فلا وبيَّنا في الطهارات من شرح الكنز بماذا تعتبر الغلبة .
The sixth: If a pure liquid mixes with absolute water, consideration is given to the predominant. If water predominates, purification with it is permissible; otherwise not. And we have explained in the section on purifications in Sharḥ al-Kanz how predominance is determined.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This is a direct application of the maxim 'al-ightibār li l-ghālib' (consideration is for the predominant). When a pure (ṭāhir) liquid mixes with water that is mutlaq (pure and purifying), the resulting mixture is judged by what is more. If the water is the majority, the mixture retains the ruling of water: it can be used for wuḍū' etc. If the other liquid is the majority, the mixture is no longer considered water for purification. The author refers to his Sharḥ al-Kanz for the detailed method of determining 'predominance' (e.g., by volume, by effect on properties). This is a furū' of the major qā'ida 'al-ghālib'.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (mā' muṭlaq): Water that is pure in itself and purifying for others (e.g., rain, well, river)
عبرة للغالب (al-'ibra li l-ghālib): Consideration is given to the predominant element

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pure liquid mixes with absolute water, when can the mixture be used for purification?
A) Always, regardless of proportions
B) Only if water is the majority
C) Only if the liquid is the majority
D) Never, once mixed it is no longer pure

Key Takeaway
When a pure liquid mixes with water, the mixture's ruling follows the predominant component: if water dominates, it remains pure and purifying.

The Rule of Predominance in Milk Mixing and Its Exception
Application of ghālib to milk mixing and radā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida of al-ghālib to cases of milk mixing and identify the exception when two human milks mix.

Core Idea
The predominance rule governs milk mixing, but when two human milks mix, both establish prohibition without weighing their proportions.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir illustrates a key legal maxim applied to fosterage: the consideration of the predominant element in mixtures. The author presents two scenarios — one where mixing occurs with a non-fosterage substance, and another between two fosterage milks — to draw a nuanced boundary for when the rule applies and when it yields to precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize that the passage begins by stating the rule: when milk mixes with a foreign substance, the predominant component determines the legal effect. This is the qawida of 'al-mu'tabar al-ghālib' (the predominant is considered).
This rule is common in Hanafi fiqh for resolving mixtures.
2
Apply the rule to the first scenario
Analyze the first scenario: mixing human milk with water, medicine, or sheep milk. In each case, if the human milk is predominant, the fosterage prohibition is established; if the other substance is predominant, it is not. If equal, precaution dictates that both prohibitions apply.
'Sheep milk' is a placeholder for any non-human milk that does not itself cause fosterage.
3
Identify the exception
The passage then presents a second scenario: mixing two women's milks. Here the rule changes: predominance is not considered. According to the correct view, both milks independently establish prohibition, regardless of which is more.
This is because both milks are equally capable of causing fosterage (they are of equal legal potential).
4
Understand the rationale
The exception arises because when both components are of the same legal kind, each retains its full effect. The rule of predominance only applies when one component is legally irrelevant (e.g., water) or of a different category (e.g., animal milk).
The author references his own work on radā' for further detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابعة : لو اختلط لبن المرأة بماء أو بدواء أو بلبن شاة فالمعتبر الغالب وتثبت الحرمة إذا استويا احتياطا كما في الغاية. واختلف فيما إذا اختلط لبن امرأة بلبن أخرى والصحيح ثبوت الحرمة فيهما من غير اعتبار الغلبة كما بيناه في الرضاع.
The seventh: If a woman's milk mixes with water, or with medicine, or with sheep's milk, the predominant is considered, and the prohibition (of marriage due to fosterage) is established if they are equal, as a precaution, as in al-Ghāya. There is a difference of opinion when a woman's milk mixes with another woman's milk; the correct view is that the prohibition is established in both cases without considering predominance, as we explained in the section on radā'.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of mixing on fosterage relationships. If a woman's milk (which establishes a foster relationship) mixes with a non-human substance, the ruling follows the predominant: if her milk is more, it still establishes the prohibition; if the other substance is more, it does not. If equal, precaution dictates that both prohibitions apply. However, when two women's milks mix, the correct view (according to the author) is that both milks retain their effect regardless of proportion because each is a potential cause for fosterage. This shows an exception to the ghālib rule: when both elements are of equal legal potential, the ruling applies to both. The author refers to his own work on radā'.

Key Terms
رضاع (raḍā'): Fosterage through breastfeeding; establishes prohibitions to marriage
غالب (ghālib): The predominant or majority element in a mixture; legally decisive unless an exception applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the passage, what is the ruling when a woman's milk mixes with an equal amount of sheep milk?
A) The prohibition is established
B) The prohibition is not established
C) The prohibition is established as a precaution
D) The ruling depends on which milk was poured first

Key Takeaway
The rule of predominance applies when human milk mixes with a non-fosterage substance, but not when two human milks mix — both establish prohibition.

The Ghālib Rule for Accepting Gifts
Application of ghālib to acceptance of gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-ghālib yu'āmal' to determine the permissibility of accepting gifts based on the predominant character of the giver's wealth.

Core Idea
If most of a person's wealth is halal, his gifts are lawful; if mostly haram, avoid unless he specifies a halal source.

The Hanafi school applies a pragmatic principle in mu'āmalāt: the majority determines the ruling. This lesson examines the rule that if the gift-giver's wealth is predominantly halal, the recipient may accept without investigation. Conversely, if it is predominantly haram, the gift is impermissible unless the giver clarifies it is from a halal source like inheritance or loan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the first part of the passage: 'إذا كان غالب مال المهدي حلالاً فلا بأس بقبول هديته... وإن كان غالب ماله الحرام لا يقبلها ولا يأكل إلا إذا قال إنه حلال ورثه أو استقرضه.' Note the binary outcome based on the predominant character.
The term 'غالب' is key; it means 'most' or 'predominant'.
2
Apply to halal-majority case
If the gift-giver's wealth is mostly halal, acceptance is permissible without further investigation, unless it becomes clear that a specific gift is from haram.
The default is to assume the gift shares the same character as the whole.
3
Apply to haram-majority case
If the gift-giver's wealth is mostly haram, rejection is the default. The gift is not accepted unless the giver explicitly states it is from a halal source, such as inheritance or a loan.
The exception is conditional: the giver must say it is halal, inherited, or borrowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة : إذا كان غالب مال المهدي ،حلالاً، فلا بأس بقبول هديته وأكل ماله ما لم يتبيَّن أنه من حرام وإن كان غالب ماله الحرام لا يقبلها ولا يأكل إلا إذا قال إنه حلال ورثه أو استقرضه قال الحلواني : وكان الإمام أبو القاسم الحاكم يأخذ جوائز السلطان، والحيلة فيه أن يشتري شيئًا بمال مطلق ثم ينقده من أي مال شاء كذا رواه الثاني عن الإمام وعن الإمام أن المبتلى بطعام السلطان والظلمة يتحرى فإن وقع في قلبه حلّه قبل وأكل وإلا لا لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «استفتِ قلبك . . . .» الحديث (۱) ، وجواب الإمام فيمن فيه ورع وصفاء قلب ينظر بنور الله تعالى ويدرك بالفراسة كذا في البزازية من الكراهة .
The eighth: If most of the gift-giver's wealth is halal, there is no harm in accepting his gift and eating his wealth, as long as it does not become clear that it is from haram. And if most of his wealth is haram, he should not accept it nor eat, unless he (the giver) says it is halal, inherited, or borrowed. Al-Ḥulwānī said: Imam Abu al-Qāsim al-Ḥākim used to accept the gifts of the sultan. The legal stratagem (ḥīla) is that he buys something with absolute money and then pays from whatever money he wishes. Thus it is reported from the Imam (Abu Ḥanīfa). And from the Imam: the one afflicted with the food of the sultan or oppressors should exercise discernment (taḥarrī): if in his heart he feels it is halal, he may accept and eat; otherwise not, due to the ḥadīth 'Seek the fatwa of your heart...' And the Imam's answer regarding one who has piety and a pure heart—he sees by the light of Allah and perceives by intuition. Thus in al-Bazzāziyya from the section on karāha.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage applies the ghālib rule to the ethics of accepting gifts. The default ruling aligns with the predominant nature of the giver's wealth. If most of his income is halal, one can accept gifts from him without investigation, unless there is clear evidence it is haram. If most is haram, one should avoid, unless the giver specifically claims a halal source. The text includes a ḥīla (legal stratagem) for rulers' gifts: one may intend to buy something with his own money and then pay from the ruler's money, effectively converting it. It also mentions taḥarrī (personal scrutiny) based on the ḥadīth about consulting one's heart. This shows that in matters of piety, one may rely on inner conviction if the person is God-fearing. The source is al-Bazzāziyya.

Key Terms
غالب (ghālib): Predominant, majority
حلال (ḥalāl): Permissible according to Islamic law
حرام (ḥarām): Impermissible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on accepting a gift from someone whose wealth is predominantly haram?
A) Permitted in all cases
B) Permitted only if the giver says it is halal, inherited, or borrowed
C) Prohibited absolutely
D) Permitted if the recipient performs taḥarrī

Key Takeaway
The ghālib rule in Hanafi fiqh allows acceptance of gifts from those with predominantly halal wealth without investigation, but requires rejection in the opposite case unless a halal source is explicitly stated.

The Ḥīla (Legal Stratagem) for Rulers' Gifts
Legal stratagem for accepting gifts from rulers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid ḥīla for receiving gifts from unjust rulers by changing one’s intention from receiving a gift to a purchase.

Core Idea
A legal stratagem can transform a forbidden gift into a permissible transaction by the intention to buy with one's own money and then pay from the ruler's funds.

In situations where a ruler's gifts are tainted by haram wealth, the Hanafi school permits a ṭarīqa (way) to lawfully benefit. The text reports that Imam Abu al-Qāsim al-Ḥākim used to accept sultan's gifts by means of a ḥīla: he would intend to buy something with his own money and then pay from the ruler's gift money, effectively making it a purchase rather than a gift. This lesson examines that stratagem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Rulers often give gifts from public funds that may contain haram elements. Accepting unconditionally may involve haram. The need is to lawfully benefit while avoiding sin.
The ruler is not necessarily the owner; the wealth may be from unjust sources.
2
Learn the ḥīla
The text says: 'اشتري شيئًا بمال مطلق ثم ينقده من أي مال شاء'. The recipient first buys an item using his own absolute money (without specifying the source). Then he pays the same amount from the ruler's gift money.
The key is to form an intention to purchase rather than receive a gift.
3
Analyze the mechanism
By intending to buy, the recipient transforms the transaction into a purchase from his own wealth. The payment from the ruler's gift is a reimbursement. Since the purchase is with his own money, the item becomes halal.
This relies on the principle 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā' (matters are judged by intentions).
4
Apply to similar scenarios
This ḥīla can be used in any situation where a person needs to accept money from a tainted source but wishes to keep it halal. The recipient must have genuine intention to purchase.
The ḥīla must not involve lying or deception about the intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة : إذا كان غالب مال المهدي ،حلالاً، فلا بأس بقبول هديته وأكل ماله ما لم يتبيَّن أنه من حرام وإن كان غالب ماله الحرام لا يقبلها ولا يأكل إلا إذا قال إنه حلال ورثه أو استقرضه قال الحلواني : وكان الإمام أبو القاسم الحاكم يأخذ جوائز السلطان، والحيلة فيه أن يشتري شيئًا بمال مطلق ثم ينقده من أي مال شاء كذا رواه الثاني عن الإمام وعن الإمام أن المبتلى بطعام السلطان والظلمة يتحرى فإن وقع في قلبه حلّه قبل وأكل وإلا لا لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «استفتِ قلبك . . . .» الحديث (۱) ، وجواب الإمام فيمن فيه ورع وصفاء قلب ينظر بنور الله تعالى ويدرك بالفراسة كذا في البزازية من الكراهة .
The eighth: If most of the gift-giver's wealth is halal, there is no harm in accepting his gift and eating his wealth, as long as it does not become clear that it is from haram. This passage applies the ghālib rule to the ethics of accepting gifts. The default ruling aligns with the predominant nature of the giver's wealth. If most of his income is halal, one can accept gifts from him without investigation, unless there is clear evidence it is haram. If most is haram, one should avoid, unless the giver specifically claims a halal source. The text includes a ḥīla (legal stratagem) for rulers' gifts: one may intend to buy something with his own money and then pay from the ruler's money, effectively converting it. It also mentions taḥarrī (personal scrutiny) based on the ḥadīth about consulting one's heart. This shows that in matters of piety, one may rely on inner conviction if the person is God-fearing. Key Terms
حيلة (ḥīla): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible end while avoiding something impermissible
جوائز السلطان (jawā'iz al-sulṭān): Gifts from the ruler
مال مطلق (māl muṭlaq): Absolute money, not designated for any specific purpose

Taḥarrī (Personal Scrutiny) in Doubtful Matters
Application of taḥarrī to doubtful food

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply taḥarrī when faced with doubtful food or gifts from rulers and understand its conditions.

Core Idea
When in doubt, the God-fearing believer can rely on inner conviction as a valid method to determine permissibility.

For those who are afflicted with the food of sultans or oppressors, the Hanafi school permits taḥarrī (personal exertion to determine the ruling). The text quotes a hadith and adds that this applies to one who has piety and a pure heart. This lesson explores the concept of taḥarrī and its legal basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
The text says: 'المبتلى بطعام السلطان والظلمة' – the one afflicted by the food of the sultan or oppressors. This applies when one cannot avoid eating such food.
This is a concession for those compelled to eat.
2
Understand taḥarrī
Taḥarrī means personal exertion to determine the correct ruling based on available evidence and inner conviction. It is used when the ruling is unclear.
Not a random guess but a sincere effort.
3
Apply the hadith
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'استفت قلبك' – seek the fatwa of your heart. This hadith is used as evidence that one can rely on one's inner feeling after due consideration.
The hadith is from a longer narration: 'البر ما اطمانت إليه النفس واطمأن إليه القلب، والإثم ما حاك في النفس'.
4
Determine who qualifies
The text adds: 'في من فيه ورع وصفاء قلب ينظر بنور الله تعالى ويدرك بالفراسة' – this applies to one who has piety (wara') and a pure heart, who sees by the light of Allah and perceives by intuition. So it is not for everyone.
The Imam's answer restricts taḥarrī to the righteous.

Mixed Pigeons and Lost Property
Application of ghālib to mixed owned and unowned pigeons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qāʿida of ghālib to determine the ruling on mixed owned and unowned pigeons and state the obligations of the finder under luqṭa rules.

Core Idea
When ownership is uncertain, the ghālib rule applies: mixed unowned property is not automatically owned, but must be returned as luqṭa.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓāʾir applies the ghālib principle to a practical scenario of mixed pigeons. It illustrates how the Hanafī school uses the concept of preponderance to resolve ownership ambiguity. The furūʿ derived here demonstrate the muftī's need to balance property rights with public harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying qāʿida
Recognise that the ruling is based on the ghālib (preponderance) principle: when owned and unowned items mix, the preponderance of owned items does not automatically transfer ownership to the unowned.
The passage explicitly states the ruling: 'la taḥrimu wa innamā tukrah'.
2
State the specific ruling
Mixed unowned pigeons are not ḥarām, but it is makrūh to take them. This balances caution with the default permissibility of unowned items.
If taken, the finder must seek the owner as with any luqṭa.
3
Apply the furūʿ from the text
The text gives two sub‑rulings: (1) the owner of the tower must feed and maintain all pigeons to prevent harm; (2) if a neighbour's pigeon mixes in, it cannot be taken without broadcasting for the owner.
These furūʿ extend the principle to practical obligations.
4
Generalise to similar cases
Use the ghālib rule to resolve other mixed‑ownership problems (e.g., pet birds, livestock, modern drones). The key is that ownership is not automatically transferred by physical mixing.
Always check if the item has a known owner – if so, it becomes luqṭa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسعة : إذا اختلطت حمامة المملوك بغير المملوك فظاهر كلامهم أنه لا تحرم وإنما تُكره قال في البزازية من اللقطة اتخذ برج حمام في قرية فينبغي : أن يحفظها ويعلفها ولا يتركها بلا علف كيلا يتضرر الناس فإن اختلط حمام غير صاحبها لا ينبغي له أن يأخذها ولو أخذها طلب صاحبها كالضالة إلى آخر ما فيها .
The ninth: If a owned pigeon mixes with an unowned pigeon, the apparent meaning of their statements is that it is not unlawful (ḥarām), but only disliked (makrūh). He said in al-Bazzāziyya from the section on luqṭa (lost property): If one builds a pigeon tower in a village, he must guard them and feed them, and not leave them without food so that people are not harmed. If a pigeon belonging to someone else mixes, he should not take it, and if he takes it, he must seek its owner like a lost item... to the end of that section.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage applies the ghālib rule to a practical scenario: a man owns pigeons that mix with wild or unowned pigeons. The fuqahā' rule that the mixed unowned pigeons are not automatically considered his property (and thus not ḥarām to take), but it is disliked to take them because of the possibility of confusion. The text refers to the responsibility of the pigeon-owner to feed and maintain them to prevent harm to neighbours. If a neighbour's pigeon enters the tower, it must be treated as a luqṭa (lost item) and returned. This ruling is based on the consideration that the majority of pigeons in the tower are likely owned, but taking a stray is not automatically theft.

Key Terms
لقطة (luqṭa): Lost property that must be returned to its owner if known
غالب (ghālib): The preponderant; used in qawāʿid to determine rulings when certainty is lacking

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pigeon belonging to someone else mixes with one's own pigeons, what should one do?
A) Keep it as his own
B) Release it
C) Seek the owner as with a lost item
D) Kill it

Key Takeaway
When owned and unowned pigeons mix, the unowned are not automatically owned; taking them is makrūh and they must be returned as lost property.

The Qā'ida of Dominant Suspicion (Ghalabat al-Ẓann) in Market Transactions
Application of ghālib to market transactions when corruption is dominant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qā'ida of ghalabat al-ẓann to determine permissibility of market purchases and identify when knowledge of specific corruption changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Dominant suspicion of corruption does not invalidate a purchase, but certain knowledge of specific corruption does.

This passage from Al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir illustrates a key legal maxim: when corruption is widespread in the market but not certain in each transaction, the default permissibility of sale remains. The text also provides concrete examples—brokerage, butchers, and gambling—to show where custom or specific knowledge alters the ruling. We will unpack the principle and its practical applications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the qā'ida
Identify the core rule: if one's dominant suspicion is that most market sales are corrupt, he should abstain, but if he buys, the purchase is permissible.
The permissibility is due to the default validity of contracts (aṣl al-ṣiḥḥa).
2
Identify the scope
This qā'ida applies to situations where corruption is only suspected, not known. It governs mu'āmalāt transactions.
It interacts with the principle of considering custom (al-'urf).
3
Work through the furū'āt
Apply the qā'ida to the three examples: broker's walnuts (commission allowed by custom), butcher's meat (owner consent presumed), and gambler's eggs/walnuts (known forbidden).
The third example is an exception due to certain knowledge.
4
Identify the istithnā'
Where there is definite knowledge that the item is from gambling, the purchase is not permissible. This shows that certainty overrides the dominant suspicion rule.
Knowledge here is 'ilm, not ẓann.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشرة : قال في القنية من الكراهة : غلب على ظنه أن أكثر بياعات أهل السوق لا تخلو عن الفساد فإن كان الغالب هو الحرام تنزّه عن شرائه ولكن مع هذا لو اشتراه يطيب له . (انتهى). وقدمناه في المبحث الثالث من قاعدة اعتبار العُرْف، ثم قال : ولا بأس بشراء جوز الدلال الذي يعدّ الجوز فيأخذ عن كل ألف عشرة، وشراء لحم السلاحين إذا كان المالك راضيًا بذلك عادة، ولا يجوز شراء بيض المقامرين المكسرة وجوزاتهم إذا عرف أنه أخذها قمارًا . .(انتهى).
The tenth: He said in al-Qunyah from the section on karāha: If it becomes his dominant suspicion that most sales of the market people are not free from corruption, if the predominant is the haram, he should abstain from purchasing, but nevertheless if he purchases it, it is permissible for him. End. And we presented it in the third discussion of the qā'ida of considering custom. Then he said: There is no harm in buying the walnuts of the broker who counts walnuts and takes ten per thousand, and buying the meat of butchers if the owner is customarily satisfied with that. And it is not permissible to buy the broken eggs of gamblers and their walnuts if he knows that he took them by gambling. End.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage shows the interplay between ghālib and personal responsibility. If a person's dominant suspicion (ghalabat al-ẓann) is that most market transactions are tainted by corruption, then it is recommended to avoid buying. However, if he does buy, the purchase is considered permissible because the default permissibility of transaction is not overridden by mere suspicion. The text then gives examples: buying from a broker who takes a commission (jūz al-dallāl) is permissible due to custom. Buying meat from butchers is also permissible if they customarily act as agents. However, buying gambling items (eggs and walnuts) is not permissible if one knows they were won through gambling. This illustrates that the ghālib rule is tempered by knowledge: if there is certainty or strong knowledge of a specific haram, the ruling changes.

Key Terms
غلب على ظنه (ghalaba 'alā ẓannihi): It became his dominant suspicion (more likely than not).
الدلال (al-dallāl): Broker or middleman who facilitates sales.
السلاحين (al-sallāḥīn): Butchers (those who slaughter and sell meat).
المقامرين (al-muqāmirīn): Gamblers (those who play games of chance for stakes).
القنية (al-Qunyah): A Hanafi fatwa compilation by Imam al-Zāhidī (later Hanafi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person knows that a specific item was obtained through gambling, what is the ruling on buying it?
A) Permissible because the act of gambling does not affect the item
B) Permissible if the price is fair
C) Not permissible because of certain knowledge of the illicit means
D) Not permissible only if the seller is a known gambler

Key Takeaway
Dominant suspicion of market corruption does not invalidate a purchase, but certain knowledge of a specific haram origin prohibits it.

Mixing Halal and Haram in Contracts: The Ghālib Rule Applied
Comprehensive application of ghālib to mixing halal and haram in various contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ghālib rule to cases where halal and haram are combined in a single contract, distinguishing between strong and weak haram and stating the resulting ruling.

Core Idea
When halal and haram combine in one contract, the strength of the haram determines whether the entire contract fails or the halal part survives.

This supplement from al-Ashbah wa al-Naẓā'ir refines the principle that when a contract mixes lawful and unlawful elements, the outcome hinges on the nature of the prohibition. Through concrete cases in marriage, dower, khul', and sale, the rule is unpacked: a strong haram (not considered property) invalidates the entire contract, while a weak haram (property) allows the halal part to stand. Understanding this distinction is crucial for issuing correct fatwas in complex transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixture in the contract
Determine what elements are combined: halal and haram. For example, in marriage, a lawful woman and an unlawful woman; in sale, permissible goods and a carcass.
2
Classify the nature of the haram element
Is the haram fundamentally prohibited (e.g., carrion, free person) or merely defective (e.g., mudabbar slave)? The former is 'strong' and the latter is 'weak'.
Strong haram is not considered property (māl); weak haram is property but still prohibited.
3
Check if the prohibition is on the combination itself
If the unlawful element is prohibited because of the combination (e.g., marrying two sisters), then the entire contract is void. Otherwise, apply the ghālib rule.
Example: marrying two sisters in one contract invalidates both, regardless of the strength.
4
Apply the ghālib rule
If the haram is strong, its invalidity spreads to the halal part, voiding the whole contract. If the haram is weak, the halal part remains valid and the haram part is removed.
In mahr and khul', the rule applies: permissible amount stands, impermissible is null.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة : يدخل في هذه القاعدة ما إذا جمع بين حلال وحرام في عقد أو نيَّة، ويدخل ذلك في أبواب منها النكاح ؛ قالوا لو جمع بين مَن تحلّ ومَن لا تحلّ كمحرمة ومجوسية، ووثنية وحليلة ومنكوحة ومعتدة ،ومحرمة صح نكاح الحلال اتفاقا. وإنما الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في انقسام المسمّى من المهر وعدمه وهي في الهداية. وليس منه ما إذا جمع بين خمس أو أختين في عقد واحد فإنه يبطل في الكل لأن المحرم الجمع لا إحداهنَّ أو أحديهما فقط. وكذا لو تزوّج أمة وحرَّة معا في عقد بطل فيهما . ومنها المهر؛ فإذا سمى ما يحلّ وما يحرم كأن تزوّجها على عشرة دراهم ودن من خمر فلها العشرة وبطل الخمر. ومنها الخلع كالمهر ففيهما غلب الحلال الحرام لما أن اشتراطه بمنزلة الشرط الفاسد وهما لا يبطلان ،به، وأما إذا زوّج الولي الصغير بأكثر من مهر المثل فإن كان أبا أو جَدًّا صح عليه وإلا فسد النكاح وقيل يصح بمهر المثل. ومنها البيع ؛ فإذا جمع بين حلال وحرام صفقة واحدة؛ فإن كان الحرام ليس بمال كالجمع بين الذكية والميتة والحرّ والعبد فإنه يسري البطلان إلى الحلال لقوة بطلان الحرام، وكذا إذا جمع بين خل وخمر. وإن كان الحرام ضعيفا كأن يكون مالاً في الجملة كما إذا جمع بين المدبر والقن أو بين القن والمكاتب أو ألم الولد أو عبد غيره؛ فإنه لا يسري الفساد إلى القن لضعفه واختلف فيما إذا جمع بين وقف
Supplement: What is included in this qā'ida is when one combines between halal and haram in a contract or intention. This enters into various chapters. From them: marriage. They said: If one combines between a woman who is permissible and one who is not permissible, such as a mahram and a Majūsiyya, an idolatress and a wife, a married woman and a woman in 'idda, and a mahram—the marriage of the permissible one is valid by agreement. The only difference between the Imām (Abū Ḥanīfa) and his two companions is concerning the division of the specified dower, as in al-Hidāya. But it does not include combining five wives or two sisters in one contract, because that invalidates all, since the prohibition is on the combination itself, not on one of them. Similarly, if one marries a slave woman and a free woman together in one contract, both are invalid. From them: the mahr. If one specifies something permissible and something impermissible, e.g., marrying on a dower of ten dirhams and a jug of wine, then she gets the ten dirhams and the wine is void. From them: khul' is like mahr: in both, the permissible overcomes the impermissible because stipulating it is like a corrupt condition that does not invalidate [the contract]. But if the guardian of a minor gives him in marriage for more than the mahr al-mithl, then if the guardian is the father or grandfather, it is valid and binding; otherwise, the marriage is invalid, and some say it is valid with mahr al-mithl. From them: sale. If one combines halal and haram in a single sale, then if the haram is not considered property (māl), such as combining a slaughtered animal and a carcass, or a free man and a slave, the invalidity spreads to the halal due to the strength of the invalidity of the haram. Similarly, if one combines vinegar and wine. But if the haram is weak, such as when it is property in some sense, like combining a mudabbar and a slave, or a slave and a kitāba, or a freedman or another's slave, then the corruption does not spread to the slave due to its weakness. There is a difference of opinion when one combines between a waqf...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This supplement is a rich application of the ghālib rule and the principle that when halal and haram are mixed in a single contract, the outcome depends on the 'strength' of the haram element. In marriage, if a man marries a lawful woman along with an unlawful one in one contract, the lawful marriage is valid, but the distribution of the mahr is disputed. However, if the combination itself is the cause of prohibition (like two sisters), then the entire contract fails. In mahr, specifying a permissible and impermissible item results in the permissible mahr being due and the impermissible void; the marriage is valid. Khul' (divorce for compensation) follows the same rule. In sales, if the haram item is not considered property (e.g., carrion, free person), the sale is entirely void. If the haram item is property but the prohibition is due to weakness (e.g., a mudabbar slave cannot be sold but is still property), the sale is valid for the halal part. The passage illustrates that the ghālib rule is not uniform; the nature of the haram (rūkn) matters. The final line mentions waqf, which continues in the next page.

Key Terms
صفقة واحدة (ṣafqa wāḥida): A single transaction that includes multiple items
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death
قاعدة الغالب (qā'idat al-ghālib): The principle that the predominant factor determines the ruling when mixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a single sale contract combining halal and haram, if the haram item is not considered property (e.g., carrion), what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void only for the haram part
B) The sale is valid for the halal part
C) The invalidity spreads to the halal part as well
D) The sale is valid entirely with a discount

Key Takeaway
The survival of the halal part in a mixed contract depends on whether the haram is fundamentally prohibited (strong) or merely defective (weak), and on whether the prohibition is on the combination itself.

15 min
Mixing Halal and Haram in Marriage and Sale Contracts
Applications of the mixing-halal-haram maxim in nikah, mahr, khul', and buyu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'When halal and haram are combined in a contract' to marriage and sale contracts, distinguishing cases where invalidity spreads based on the strength of the haram element.

Core Idea
If the haram is not property (e.g. carcass), it voids the whole contract; if it is weak property, the halal part survives.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi legal maxim: when a contract combines a halal and a haram element, the outcome depends on the nature of the haram. The passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair applies this to marriage (nikah), dower (mahr), divorce by khul', and sale (bay'). We will work through these applications to see how the strength or weakness of the haram determines whether the invalidity spreads.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
Read the core statement: 'When halal and haram are combined in a contract or intention.' This maxim applies across many chapters. Focus on the rule that the strength of the haram determines whether the invalidity spreads.
2
Analyze marriage applications
Note the rule: if a permissible woman is combined with an impermissible one in the same contract, the permissible marriage is valid by agreement. Then, distinguish the case of combining five women or two sisters (void in all) because the prohibition is on the combination itself.
The mahr division dispute between Imam and Sahibayn is a separate nuance.
3
Examine mahr and khul' cases
When the dower includes a permissible part (dirhams) and a prohibited part (wine), the permissible part is valid and the prohibited portion is void. Similarly for khul', the permissible overcomes the prohibited.
4
Analyze sale transactions
For sale, if the haram is not property (e.g. carcass, free person), the invalidity spreads to the halal part. If the haram is weak property (e.g. mudabbar, mukatab), the halal part remains valid. Also consider the case of combining waqf and owned property.
5
Identify the key distinction
The decisive factor is whether the haram element is considered 'property' (mal) or not. Non-property items have strong invalidity; weak property items have weak invalidity, so the halal part survives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشرة : قال في القنية من الكراهة : غلب على ظنه أن أكثر بياعات أهل السوق لا تخلو عن الفساد فإن كان الغالب هو الحرام تنزّه عن شرائه ولكن مع هذا لو اشتراه يطيب له . (انتهى). وقدمناه في المبحث الثالث من قاعدة اعتبار العُرْف، ثم قال : ولا بأس بشراء جوز الدلال الذي يعدّ الجوز فيأخذ عن كل ألف عشرة، وشراء لحم السلاحين إذا كان المالك راضيًا بذلك عادة، ولا يجوز شراء بيض المقامرين المكسرة وجوزاتهم إذا عرف أنه أخذها قمارًا . .(انتهى). أما مسألة الخلط فمذكورة بأقسامها في البزازية من الوديعة. وأما مسألة ما إذا اختلط الحلال بالحرام في البلد فإنه يجوز الشراء والأخذ إلا أن تقوم دلالة على أنه من الحرام، كذا في الأصل.
تتمة :
يدخل في هذه القاعدة ما إذا جمع بين حلال وحرام في عقد أو نيَّة، ويدخل ذلك في أبواب منها النكاح ؛ قالوا لو جمع بين مَن تحلّ ومَن لا تحلّ كمحرمة ومجوسية، ووثنية وحليلة ومنكوحة ومعتدة ،ومحرمة صح نكاح الحلال اتفاقا. وإنما الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في انقسام المسمّى من المهر وعدمه وهي في الهداية. وليس منه ما إذا جمع بين خمس أو أختين في عقد واحد فإنه يبطل في الكل لأن المحرم الجمع لا إحداهنَّ أو أحديهما فقط. وكذا لو تزوّج أمة وحرَّة معا في عقد بطل فيهما .
ومنها المهر؛ فإذا سمى ما يحلّ وما يحرم كأن تزوّجها على عشرة دراهم ودن من خمر فلها العشرة وبطل الخمر.
ومنها الخلع كالمهر ففيهما غلب الحلال الحرام لما أن اشتراطه بمنزلة الشرط الفاسد وهما لا يبطلان ،به، وأما إذا زوّج الولي الصغير بأكثر من مهر المثل فإن كان أبا أو جَدًّا صح عليه وإلا فسد النكاح وقيل يصح بمهر المثل.
ومنها البيع ؛ فإذا جمع بين حلال وحرام صفقة واحدة؛ فإن كان الحرام ليس بمال كالجمع بين الذكية والميتة والحرّ والعبد فإنه يسري البطلان إلى الحلال لقوة بطلان الحرام، وكذا إذا جمع بين خل وخمر. وإن كان الحرام ضعيفا كأن يكون مالاً في الجملة كما إذا جمع بين المدبر والقن أو بين القن والمكاتب أو ألم الولد أو عبد غيره؛ فإنه لا يسري الفساد إلى القن لضعفه واختلف فيما إذا جمع بين وقف وملك، والأصح أنه لا الفساد إلى الملك لأن الوقف مال نعم إذا كان مسجدا يسري عامرًا فهو كالحرّ بخلاف الغامر بالمعجمة أي الخراب فكالمدبر. ومن هذا القبيل ما إذا شرط الخيار فيه أكثر من ثلاثة فإنه لا يصح في الثلاثة ويبطل فيما زاد، بل يبطل في الكل، لكن إذا أسقط الزائد قبل دخوله انقلب البيع صحيحًا .
ومنها ما إذا جمع بين مجهول ومعلوم في البيع فإن كان المجهول لا تُفضي جهالته إلى المنازعة لا يضرّ وإلا فسد في الكل كما علم في البيوع . ومنها الإجارة ؛ فهي كالبيع لاشتراكهما في أنهما يبطلان بالشرط الفاسد وصرَّحوا بأنه لو استأجر دارًا في كل شهر بكذا فإنه يصح في الشهر الأول فقط . ولم أرَ الآن حكم ما إذا استأجر نساجًا لينسج له ثوبًا طوله كذا، وعرضه كذا فخالف بزيادة أو نقصان. هل يستحق بقدره أو لا يستحق أصلا؟
ومنها الكفالة والإبراء وينبغي أن لا يتعدى إلى الجائز. وقالوا لو قال لها ضمنت لك نفقتك كل شهر فإنه يصح في شهر واحد.
ومنها الهبة وهي لا تبطل بالشرط الفاسد فلا يتعدى إلى الجائز .
ومنها الإهداء ؛ قالوا لو أهدي إلى القاضي من له عادة بالإهداء له قبل القضاء وزاد، يردّ القاضي الزائد لا الكل كما في فتح القدير، فلم يتعد إلى الجائز. وظاهر كلامه أنه إن زاد في القدر. وأما إذا زاد في المعنى كما إذا كانت عادته إهداء ثوب كتان فأهدى ثوبا حريرًا لم أره الآن لأصحابنا رحمهم الله. وينبغي وجوب ردّ الكل لا بقدر ما زاد في قيمته لعدم تمييزها من الجائز .
ومنها الوصية ؛ فلو أوصى لأجنبي ووارثه فللأجنبي نصفها وبطلت للوارث؛ كما في الكنز، وكذا لو أوصى للقاتل وللأجنبي.
ومنها الإقرار ؛ قال الزيلعي فيما لو أقر بعين أو دين لوارثه ولأجنبي لم يصح في حق الأجنبي أيضًا. (انتهى). وفي المجمع من الإقرار لو أقر لوارث مع أجنبي فتكاذبا الشركة صححه في الأجنبي. (انتهى) .
ومنها باب الشهادة، فإذا جمع فيها بين مَن تجوز شهادته ومَن لا تجوز؛ ففي الظهيرية منها : رجل مات وأوصى لفقراء جيرانه بشيء وأنكرت الورثة وصيته فشهد على الوصية رجلان من جيرانه لهما أولاد محاويج . قال محمد رحمه الله : لا تقبل شهادتهما لأنهما شهدا لأولادهما فيما يخص أولادهما فبطلت شهادتهما في ذلك، فإذا
The tenth: He said in al-Qunyah from the chapter of dislike: If he strongly supposes that most sales of the market are not free from corruption, and if the prevalent is the haram, he should abstain from purchasing it, but nevertheless if he purchases it, it becomes permissible for him. (End). We have presented it in the third section of the maxim of considering custom. Then he said: There is no harm in purchasing the broker's nuts who counts nuts and takes ten from every thousand, and purchasing the meat of butchers if the owner is customarily pleased with that, and it is not permissible to purchase broken eggs of gamblers and their nuts if it is known that he took them by gambling. (End). As for the issue of mixing, it is mentioned with its divisions in al-Bazzaziyyah from the chapter of deposit. As for the issue of when halal and haram are mixed in the town, it is permissible to purchase and take except if there is an indication that it is from the haram. Thus in al-Asl.
Appendix:
This maxim includes when someone combines halal and haram in a contract or intention, and this enters into various chapters. Among them is marriage: They said if someone combines a woman who is permissible and one who is not, such as a mahram and a Magian, an idolatress and a wife, a married woman and a woman in iddah, and a mahram, the marriage of the permissible one is valid by agreement. The only disagreement between the Imam and his two companions is regarding whether the specified dower is divided or not, and it is in al-Hidayah. Not included is when someone combines five [women] or two sisters in one contract, for then it is void in all, because the prohibition is against combining, not against one of them. Similarly, if he marries a slave woman and a free woman together in one contract, it is void in both.
Among them is the dower: If he specifies what is permissible and what is prohibited, such as marrying her for ten dirhams and a jug of wine, then she gets the ten dirhams and the wine is void.
Among them is khul' like the dower, so in both of them the permissible overcomes the prohibited, because stipulating it is like an invalid condition, and both do not invalidate [the contract] by it. As for when the guardian marries a minor for more than the proper dower, if he is a father or grandfather, it is valid against him; otherwise, the marriage is void, and it is said it is valid at the proper dower.
Among them is sale: If someone combines halal and haram in one transaction, then if the haram is not property, like combining ritually slaughtered and carcass, or a free person and a slave, the invalidity spreads to the permissible due to the strength of the invalidity of the haram. Similarly, if he combines vinegar and wine. If the haram is weak, for example if it is property in a general sense, as if he combines a mudabbar and a qinn, or a qinn and a mukatab, or umm walad, or another's slave, then the corruption does not spread to the qinn due to its weakness. There is disagreement regarding combining a waqf and owned property; the most correct is that it does not spread to the owned property because waqf is property. Yes, if it is a mosque, it spreads if it is populated ('amir), like the free person, contrary to the desolate (ghamir, i.e., ruined), which is like the mudabbar. Similar to this is when he stipulates an option for more than three days; it is not valid for three days and becomes void for the excess, rather it becomes void in all, but if he drops the excess before it enters, the sale becomes valid.
Among them is combining unknown and known in sale: if the unknown does not lead to dispute, it does not harm; otherwise, it becomes void in all, as is known in sales. Among them is hire: it is like sale because they both become void by an invalid condition. They explicitly stated that if he rents a house for each month for a certain amount, it is valid for the first month only. I have not now seen the ruling if he hires a weaver to weave him a cloth of such-and-such length and width, and he deviates by increase or decrease: does he deserve [wages] according to the measure or does he not deserve anything at all?
Among them is suretyship and release, and it should not extend to that which is permissible. They said: if he says to her, 'I guarantee your maintenance every month,' then it is valid for one month.
Among them is gift, which is not nullified by an invalid condition, so it does not extend to the permissible.
Among them is gifting: They said if someone gives a gift to a judge who had a custom of giving gifts to him before judgment and increases, the judge returns the excess, not the whole, as in Fath al-Qadir, so it did not extend to the permissible. The apparent meaning of his words is that he increased in quantity. As for increasing in essence, such as if his custom was to gift a linen garment and he gifted a silk garment, I have not now seen [a ruling] from our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them. It seems it is obligatory to return the whole, not just the excess in value, because it cannot be distinguished from the permissible.
Among them is bequest: If he bequeaths to a stranger and to his heir, then the stranger gets half, and the bequest to the heir is void, as in al-Kanz. Similarly, if he bequeaths to a killer and to a stranger.
Among them is acknowledgment: al-Zayla'i said regarding one who acknowledges a specific item or debt to his heir and a stranger, it is not valid for the stranger either. (End). In al-Majma' from the chapter of acknowledgment: if he acknowledges to an heir along with a stranger and they both deny the partnership, it is validated for the stranger. (End).
Among them is the chapter of testimony: If one combines in it someone whose testimony is acceptable and someone whose is not, then in al-Zahiriyyah: A man died and bequeathed something to his poor neighbors, and the heirs denied his bequest. Two men from among the neighbors testified to the bequest, and they had needy children. Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Their testimony is not accepted because they testified for their children regarding what pertains to their children, so their testimony is void in that.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a legal maxim (qa'ida) regarding the mixing of halal and haram in contracts or intentions. The author begins by citing a ruling from al-Qunyah about market transactions where most sales are corrupt: if the prevalent is haram, one should avoid buying, but if bought, it is permissible. This is linked to the maxim of considering custom ('urf). Then he mentions specific cases: buying from brokers or butchers where custom allows, but not buying from gamblers when known. Next, the core maxim is introduced: when halal and haram are combined in a contract or intention, various rulings apply across fiqh chapters.

In marriage: combining a permissible woman with an impermissible one does not invalidate the marriage of the permissible by agreement. The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is whether the dower is apportioned. However, combining five women or two sisters in one contract voids both, as does marrying a slave and free woman together.

In dower and khul': specifying a permissible and prohibited component (e.g., dirhams and wine) results in the permissible component being valid and the prohibited one void. For khul', the permissible overcomes the prohibited.

In sale: if the haram is not property (e.g., carcass, free person), the invalidity spreads to the halal part. If the haram is weak (e.g., mudabbar, mukatab), it does not spread. For waqf and owned property, the correct view is no spread unless the waqf is a populated mosque, which is like a free person. The option stipulation beyond three days voids the whole sale but can be cured by dropping the excess.

In hire: it's like sale; an invalid condition voids only the first month in a multi-month contract. The author notes he hasn't seen a ruling on deviation in weaving.

In suretyship and release: the rule does not extend to permissible acts. A monthly guarantee is valid for one month.

In gift: invalid conditions do not nullify the gift, so no spread.

In giving gifts to a judge: if the quantity increases, only the excess is returned; if the essence changes (e.g., silk instead of linen), the author suggests returning the whole.

In bequest: if to a stranger and an heir, the heir's portion is void, half goes to the stranger.

In acknowledgment: generally invalid for the stranger if alongside an heir, but in some cases it is validated.

In testimony: if one testifies for one's own children, it is void, so the whole testimony is invalid.

The passage demonstrates the maxim's application across many areas, emphasizing the strength of the haram component as the key factor.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose freedom is deferred until the master's death
القن (al-qinn): A full slave who is owned outright
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master for freedom in exchange for payments
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who bears her master's child
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment, property dedicated for charitable purposes

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a single sale transaction, a person combines a ritually slaughtered animal (halal) with a carcass (haram). What is the ruling according to the maxim discussed?
A) The sale is valid for the halal portion only.
B) The invalidity of the haram spreads to the halal, voiding the entire sale.
C) The sale is valid for both because the halal overcomes the haram.
D) The sale is void only for the carcass, but the halal part is sold separately.

Key Takeaway
The strength of the haram element determines whether invalidity spreads to the halal part: non-property haram voids all; weak property haram leaves the halal intact.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
15 min
Mixing Halal and Haram in Hire, Gift, Bequest, and Other Contracts
Applications of the mixing-halal-haram maxim in ijarah, kafalah, hiba, ihda', wasiyyah, iqrar, shahadah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'When halal and haram are combined in a contract' to hire, gift, bequest, acknowledgment, and testimony cases, identifying when the haram element spreads or is contained.

Core Idea
In hire and guarantee, invalidity is limited to the first unit; in gifts and gifts to judges, only the excess is problematic.

This lesson continues exploring the Hanafi maxim on mixing halal and haram, focusing on contracts beyond marriage and sale. The passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair applies the principle to hire (ijarah), suretyship (kafalah), gift (hiba), gifting to judges (ihda'), bequest (wasiyyah), acknowledgment (iqrar), and testimony (shahadah). Each application demonstrates how the maxim operates with nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim's application to hire and suretyship
In hire: if a contract specifies a monthly rent for an indefinite period, only the first month is valid because combining valid and invalid periods causes the invalidity to be contained. Similarly, for a monthly maintenance guarantee, only one month is valid.
The rule: the invalid terms do not spread beyond the first unit.
2
Analyze gift and gifting to a judge
Gift (hiba) is not invalidated by an invalid condition, so mixing does not spread. For gifting to a judge: if the quantity increases beyond custom, only the excess is returned; if the essence changes (e.g., silk instead of linen), the entire gift may need to be returned because the essence is distinguishable.
3
Examine bequest and acknowledgment
If a bequest combines a stranger and an heir, the heir's portion is void and the stranger gets half. In acknowledgment: if one acknowledges a debt to an heir and a stranger, the stranger's part is invalid according to al-Zaylai; but in al-Majma', if they deny partnership, the stranger's acknowledgment is validated.
4
Consider testimony
If witnesses testify for their own children (who are heirs), their testimony is void because they have a personal interest. The invalidity spreads to the whole testimony.
5
Synthesize the common thread
Across all contracts, the maxim is applied by assessing whether the haram element is intrinsic to the contract or merely incidental. If incidental, it is contained; if intrinsic, it spreads.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشرة : قال في القنية من الكراهة : غلب على ظنه أن أكثر بياعات أهل السوق لا تخلو عن الفساد فإن كان الغالب هو الحرام تنزّه عن شرائه ولكن مع هذا لو اشتراه يطيب له . (انتهى). وقدمناه في المبحث الثالث من قاعدة اعتبار العُرْف، ثم قال : ولا بأس بشراء جوز الدلال الذي يعدّ الجوز فيأخذ عن كل ألف عشرة، وشراء لحم السلاحين إذا كان المالك راضيًا بذلك عادة، ولا يجوز شراء بيض المقامرين المكسرة وجوزاتهم إذا عرف أنه أخذها قمارًا . .(انتهى). أما مسألة الخلط فمذكورة بأقسامها في البزازية من الوديعة. وأما مسألة ما إذا اختلط الحلال بالحرام في البلد فإنه يجوز الشراء والأخذ إلا أن تقوم دلالة على أنه من الحرام، كذا في الأصل.
تتمة :
يدخل في هذه القاعدة ما إذا جمع بين حلال وحرام في عقد أو نيَّة، ويدخل ذلك في أبواب منها النكاح ؛ قالوا لو جمع بين مَن تحلّ ومَن لا تحلّ كمحرمة ومجوسية، ووثنية وحليلة ومنكوحة ومعتدة ،ومحرمة صح نكاح الحلال اتفاقا. وإنما الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في انقسام المسمّى من المهر وعدمه وهي في الهداية. وليس منه ما إذا جمع بين خمس أو أختين في عقد واحد فإنه يبطل في الكل لأن المحرم الجمع لا إحداهنَّ أو أحديهما فقط. وكذا لو تزوّج أمة وحرَّة معا في عقد بطل فيهما .
ومنها المهر؛ فإذا سمى ما يحلّ وما يحرم كأن تزوّجها على عشرة دراهم ودن من خمر فلها العشرة وبطل الخمر.
ومنها الخلع كالمهر ففيهما غلب الحلال الحرام لما أن اشتراطه بمنزلة الشرط الفاسد وهما لا يبطلان ،به، وأما إذا زوّج الولي الصغير بأكثر من مهر المثل فإن كان أبا أو جَدًّا صح عليه وإلا فسد النكاح وقيل يصح بمهر المثل.
ومنها البيع ؛ فإذا جمع بين حلال وحرام صفقة واحدة؛ فإن كان الحرام ليس بمال كالجمع بين الذكية والميتة والحرّ والعبد فإنه يسري البطلان إلى الحلال لقوة بطلان الحرام، وكذا إذا جمع بين خل وخمر. وإن كان الحرام ضعيفا كأن يكون مالاً في الجملة كما إذا جمع بين المدبر والقن أو بين القن والمكاتب أو ألم الولد أو عبد غيره؛ فإنه لا يسري الفساد إلى القن لضعفه واختلف فيما إذا جمع بين وقف وملك، والأصح أنه لا الفساد إلى الملك لأن الوقف مال نعم إذا كان مسجدا يسري عامرًا فهو كالحرّ بخلاف الغامر بالمعجمة أي الخراب فكالمدبر. ومن هذا القبيل ما إذا شرط الخيار فيه أكثر من ثلاثة فإنه لا يصح في الثلاثة ويبطل فيما زاد، بل يبطل في الكل، لكن إذا أسقط الزائد قبل دخوله انقلب البيع صحيحًا .
ومنها ما إذا جمع بين مجهول ومعلوم في البيع فإن كان المجهول لا تُفضي جهالته إلى المنازعة لا يضرّ وإلا فسد في الكل كما علم في البيوع . ومنها الإجارة ؛ فهي كالبيع لاشتراكهما في أنهما يبطلان بالشرط الفاسد وصرَّحوا بأنه لو استأجر دارًا في كل شهر بكذا فإنه يصح في الشهر الأول فقط . ولم أرَ الآن حكم ما إذا استأجر نساجًا لينسج له ثوبًا طوله كذا، وعرضه كذا فخالف بزيادة أو نقصان. هل يستحق بقدره أو لا يستحق أصلا؟
ومنها الكفالة والإبراء وينبغي أن لا يتعدى إلى الجائز. وقالوا لو قال لها ضمنت لك نفقتك كل شهر فإنه يصح في شهر واحد.
ومنها الهبة وهي لا تبطل بالشرط الفاسد فلا يتعدى إلى الجائز .
ومنها الإهداء ؛ قالوا لو أهدي إلى القاضي من له عادة بالإهداء له قبل القضاء وزاد، يردّ القاضي الزائد لا الكل كما في فتح القدير، فلم يتعد إلى الجائز. وظاهر كلامه أنه إن زاد في القدر. وأما إذا زاد في المعنى كما إذا كانت عادته إهداء ثوب كتان فأهدى ثوبا حريرًا لم أره الآن لأصحابنا رحمهم الله. وينبغي وجوب ردّ الكل لا بقدر ما زاد في قيمته لعدم تمييزها من الجائز .
ومنها الوصية ؛ فلو أوصى لأجنبي ووارثه فللأجنبي نصفها وبطلت للوارث؛ كما في الكنز، وكذا لو أوصى للقاتل وللأجنبي.
ومنها الإقرار ؛ قال الزيلعي فيما لو أقر بعين أو دين لوارثه ولأجنبي لم يصح في حق الأجنبي أيضًا. (انتهى). وفي المجمع من الإقرار لو أقر لوارث مع أجنبي فتكاذبا الشركة صححه في الأجنبي. (انتهى) .
ومنها باب الشهادة، فإذا جمع فيها بين مَن تجوز شهادته ومَن لا تجوز؛ ففي الظهيرية منها : رجل مات وأوصى لفقراء جيرانه بشيء وأنكرت الورثة وصيته فشهد على الوصية رجلان من جيرانه لهما أولاد محاويج . قال محمد رحمه الله : لا تقبل شهادتهما لأنهما شهدا لأولادهما فيما يخص أولادهما فبطلت شهادتهما في ذلك، فإذا
The tenth: He said in al-Qunyah from the chapter of dislike: If he strongly supposes that most sales of the market are not free from corruption, and if the prevalent is the haram, he should abstain from purchasing it, but nevertheless if he purchases it, it becomes permissible for him. (End). We have presented it in the third section of the maxim of considering custom. Then he said: There is no harm in purchasing the broker's nuts who counts nuts and takes ten from every thousand, and purchasing the meat of butchers if the owner is customarily pleased with that, and it is not permissible to purchase broken eggs of gamblers and their nuts if it is known that he took them by gambling. (End). As for the issue of mixing, it is mentioned with its divisions in al-Bazzaziyyah from the chapter of deposit. As for the issue of when halal and haram are mixed in the town, it is permissible to purchase and take except if there is an indication that it is from the haram. Thus in al-Asl.
Appendix:
This maxim includes when someone combines halal and haram in a contract or intention, and this enters into various chapters. Among them is marriage: They said if someone combines a woman who is permissible and one who is not, such as a mahram and a Magian, an idolatress and a wife, a married woman and a woman in iddah, and a mahram, the marriage of the permissible one is valid by agreement. The only disagreement between the Imam and his two companions is regarding whether the specified dower is divided or not, and it is in al-Hidayah. Not included is when someone combines five [women] or two sisters in one contract, for then it is void in all, because the prohibition is against combining, not against one of them. Similarly, if he marries a slave woman and a free woman together in one contract, it is void in both.
Among them is the dower: If he specifies what is permissible and what is prohibited, such as marrying her for ten dirhams and a jug of wine, then she gets the ten dirhams and the wine is void.
Among them is khul' like the dower, so in both of them the permissible overcomes the prohibited, because stipulating it is like an invalid condition, and both do not invalidate [the contract] by it. As for when the guardian marries a minor for more than the proper dower, if he is a father or grandfather, it is valid against him; otherwise, the marriage is void, and it is said it is valid at the proper dower.
Among them is sale: If someone combines halal and haram in one transaction, then if the haram is not property, like combining ritually slaughtered and carcass, or a free person and a slave, the invalidity spreads to the permissible due to the strength of the invalidity of the haram. Similarly, if he combines vinegar and wine. If the haram is weak, for example if it is property in a general sense, as if he combines a mudabbar and a qinn, or a qinn and a mukatab, or umm walad, or another's slave, then the corruption does not spread to the qinn due to its weakness. There is disagreement regarding combining a waqf and owned property; the most correct is that it does not spread to the owned property because waqf is property. Yes, if it is a mosque, it spreads if it is populated ('amir), like the free person, contrary to the desolate (ghamir, i.e., ruined), which is like the mudabbar. Similar to this is when he stipulates an option for more than three days; it is not valid for three days and becomes void for the excess, rather it becomes void in all, but if he drops the excess before it enters, the sale becomes valid.
Among them is combining unknown and known in sale: if the unknown does not lead to dispute, it does not harm; otherwise, it becomes void in all, as is known in sales. Among them is hire: it is like sale because they both become void by an invalid condition. They explicitly stated that if he rents a house for each month for a certain amount, it is valid for the first month only. I have not now seen the ruling if he hires a weaver to weave him a cloth of such-and-such length and width, and he deviates by increase or decrease: does he deserve [wages] according to the measure or does he not deserve anything at all?
Among them is suretyship and release, and it should not extend to that which is permissible. They said: if he says to her, 'I guarantee your maintenance every month,' then it is valid for one month.
Among them is gift, which is not nullified by an invalid condition, so it does not extend to the permissible.
Among them is gifting: They said if someone gives a gift to a judge who had a custom of giving gifts to him before judgment and increases, the judge returns the excess, not the whole, as in Fath al-Qadir, so it did not extend to the permissible. The apparent meaning of his words is that he increased in quantity. As for increasing in essence, such as if his custom was to gift a linen garment and he gifted a silk garment, I have not now seen [a ruling] from our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them. It seems it is obligatory to return the whole, not just the excess in value, because it cannot be distinguished from the permissible.
Among them is bequest: If he bequeaths to a stranger and to his heir, then the stranger gets half, and the bequest to the heir is void, as in al-Kanz. Similarly, if he bequeaths to a killer and to a stranger.
Among them is acknowledgment: al-Zayla'i said regarding one who acknowledges a specific item or debt to his heir and a stranger, it is not valid for the stranger either. (End). In al-Majma' from the chapter of acknowledgment: if he acknowledges to an heir along with a stranger and they both deny the partnership, it is validated for the stranger. (End).
Among them is the chapter of testimony: If one combines in it someone whose testimony is acceptable and someone whose is not, then in al-Zahiriyyah: A man died and bequeathed something to his poor neighbors, and the heirs denied his bequest. Two men from among the neighbors testified to the bequest, and they had needy children. Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Their testimony is not accepted because they testified for their children regarding what pertains to their children, so their testimony is void in that.

This passage is an expansion of a legal maxim (qa'ida) regarding the mixing of halal and haram in contracts or intentions. However, combining five women or two sisters in one contract voids both, as does marrying a slave and free woman together.

In sale: if the haram is not property (e.g., carcass, free person), the invalidity spreads to the halal part. If the haram is weak (e.g., mudabbar, mukatab), it does not spread. For waqf and owned property, the correct view is no spread unless the waqf is a populated mosque, which is like a free person. In testimony: if one testifies for one's own children, it is void, so the whole testimony is invalid.

The passage demonstrates the maxim's application across many areas, emphasizing the strength of the haram component as the key factor.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): Hire or lease contract
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; guarantee of a debt or obligation
الهبة (al-hiba): Gift; transfer of ownership without compensation
الإهداء (al-ihda'): Gifting, especially to a judge or official
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): Bequest; will
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment; admission of a right or debt
الشهادة (al-shahadah): Testimony; giving evidence in court

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man rents a house for '100 dirhams per month' with no end date. How many months are valid?
A) All months are valid because the price is specified per month.
B) Only the first month is valid.
C) The contract is entirely void.
D) The contract is valid for one year.

Key Takeaway
When halal and haram combine in indefinite hire, gift, or testimony, the ruling varies: sometimes only the first unit is valid, sometimes the whole is void, depending on the nature of the haram.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
12 min
Spread of Invalidity in Contracts: Sales, Ijarah, and Gifts
Applications of the maxim on spread of invalidity across contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida on spread of invalidity to mixed contracts and identify when the impermissible part does not invalidate the whole.

Core Idea
The spread of invalidity depends on indivisibility; tests: does the impermissible part taint the essence?

This lesson unpacks a key qawida: when permissible and impermissible combine in a contract, does the invalidity spread? The answer is not uniform. Through cases in sales, ijarah, kafalah, hibah, and wasiyyah, you will learn to distinguish when the contract is wholly void, partially valid, or fully valid despite the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed contract
Find the case where the text combines a permissible (ja'iz) and impermissible (ghayr ja'iz) element. For example, a sale with an option exceeding three days.
The impermissible element could be a condition, time, or subject.
2
Determine indivisibility
Ask: can the contract be separated into parts? If the impermissible element is intrinsic, the whole is invalid. If separable, only the invalid part is void.
Hibah is an exception: corrupt conditions do not invalidate the gift.
3
Apply the furu' ruling
Match the case to the explicit rulings in the text: e.g., option beyond three days → entire sale void (unless dropped). Renting monthly → valid only for first month.
Some cases, like hiring a weaver, are unresolved; note the author's hesitation.
4
Check for exceptions (istithna')
Note that hibah, zakat paid early, and tayammum are exceptions where invalidity does not spread.
These exceptions are governed by separate qawaid.
